Front,Back
Slow on the uptake,"<b>Meaning:</b> Taking a long time to understand something. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Refers to the speed at which an engine or mind 'takes up' fuel or information. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> I explained the joke twice, but he was a bit slow on the uptake."
Smell a rat,"<b>Meaning:</b> To suspect that something is wrong or that someone is being dishonest. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> A dog smelling a hidden rat even if it cannot see it. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> They offered me a free car, but I smell a rat; there must be a catch."
Smooth sailing,"<b>Meaning:</b> A situation that is easy and free of problems. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Nautical; traveling on calm waters without wind or waves. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> Once we passed the exam, it was smooth sailing to graduation."
Snug as a bug in a rug,"<b>Meaning:</b> Very comfortable, cozy, and warm. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> A 1769 poem by Benjamin Franklin. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> With the heater on and a blanket, I'm snug as a bug in a rug."
Spill the beans,"<b>Meaning:</b> To reveal a secret. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Ancient Greeks voted using white and black beans; if the jar fell, the secret results were revealed. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> We were planning a surprise, but my brother spilled the beans."
Spin a yarn,<b>Meaning:</b> To tell a long and often exaggerated story. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Sailors would tell long stories while performing the tedious task of untwisting old rope (yarn). <br><br> <b>Example:</b> Grandpa loves to spin a yarn about his days in the navy.
Spitting image,<b>Meaning:</b> A perfect resemblance; looking exactly like someone else. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Corruption of 'spirit and image.' <br><br> <b>Example:</b> He is the spitting image of his father.
Square deal,<b>Meaning:</b> A fair and honest transaction or arrangement. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Popularized by Theodore Roosevelt; 'square' refers to something being solid and even. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> All I want is a square deal from the insurance company.
Stab someone in the back,"<b>Meaning:</b> To betray someone who trusted you. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> A metaphor for a cowardly physical attack from behind. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> I thought we were friends, but you stabbed me in the back by taking my promotion."
Steer clear of,<b>Meaning:</b> To avoid someone or something. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Nautical; steering a ship away from obstacles like rocks or other boats. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> You should steer clear of that neighborhood at night.
Step up to the plate,<b>Meaning:</b> To take responsibility for something or prepare to face a challenge. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> From baseball; the batter moving to 'home plate' to face the pitcher. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> The team needs a leader; it's time for someone to step up to the plate.
Stick to your guns,"<b>Meaning:</b> To refuse to change your opinion or decision despite pressure. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> From sailors or soldiers being ordered to stay at their cannons even under heavy fire. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> Everyone disagreed with her, but she stuck to her guns."
Storm out,"<b>Meaning:</b> To leave a place quickly and angrily. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Likening a person's anger to a sudden, violent storm. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> After the argument, he stormed out of the house."
Straight and narrow,"<b>Meaning:</b> Living a law-abiding and moral life. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> From the Bible (Matthew 7:14), describing the difficult path to heaven. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> Since leaving prison, he has stayed on the straight and narrow."
Strike while the iron is hot,<b>Meaning:</b> To take action immediately while an opportunity is still available. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> From blacksmithing; iron must be hit while it is glowing hot to be shaped. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> The store is having a flash sale; strike while the iron is hot!
Swallow your pride,<b>Meaning:</b> To humble yourself and admit you were wrong or need help. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> A metaphor for 'consuming' an unpleasant feeling for the sake of a better outcome. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> I had to swallow my pride and ask my parents for money.
Take a back seat,<b>Meaning:</b> To take a less active or less important role. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Moving from the driver's seat to the passenger area of a car. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> I’m letting my assistant lead this project; I’m going to take a back seat.
Take a rain check,"<b>Meaning:</b> To politely decline an invitation but suggest doing it at a later time. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> From baseball; if a game was canceled due to rain, spectators were given a 'check' to attend a future game. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> I can't go to dinner tonight, but can I take a rain check?"
Take the cake,"<b>Meaning:</b> To be the most remarkable, foolish, or annoying example of something. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> From 19th-century 'cake walks' where the best performer won a cake. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> I've seen some bad drivers, but that guy really takes the cake."
Take with a pinch of salt,"<b>Meaning:</b> To be skeptical of a statement; to not believe it completely. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> From an ancient Roman belief that salt helped digest or neutralize poison. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> He says he met the president, but I’d take that with a pinch of salt."
Talk shop,"<b>Meaning:</b> To talk about work matters, especially in a social setting. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> From the 19th century; tradespeople discussing their 'shops.' <br><br> <b>Example:</b> We went to the party to relax, but the engineers started talking shop immediately."
The apple of discord,<b>Meaning:</b> Something that causes trouble or a great argument. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Greek mythology; a golden apple thrown into a party that led to the Trojan War. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> The inheritance became the apple of discord between the three brothers.
The bottom line,<b>Meaning:</b> The final result or the most important point. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> From accounting; the last line on a financial statement showing net profit or loss. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> The bottom line is that we don't have enough money for this project.
The coast is clear,"<b>Meaning:</b> It is safe to proceed because no one is watching. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> From smuggling; if no guards were on the beach, it was safe to land the goods. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> You can come out now; the coast is clear."
The icing on the cake,"<b>Meaning:</b> An extra benefit that makes a good situation even better. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Cake is good on its own; icing is a sweet, decorative extra. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> I got the promotion, and the pay raise was the icing on the cake."
The last straw,<b>Meaning:</b> The final problem in a series that makes a person lose their patience. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> From 'the straw that broke the camel's back.' <br><br> <b>Example:</b> My car broke down and then it started raining; that was the last straw.
The lesser of two evils,"<b>Meaning:</b> Choosing the less harmful of two bad options. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> A principle dating back to ancient Greek philosophy. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> Neither candidate is great, but I’ll vote for the lesser of two evils."
The sky is the limit,"<b>Meaning:</b> There is no limit to what can be achieved. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> A 20th-century American idiom suggesting infinite potential. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> With your talent, the sky is the limit for your career."
The whole ball of wax,"<b>Meaning:</b> Everything; the entire thing. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Disputed; possibly from old legal land divisions. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> We sold the house, the car, and the furniture—the whole ball of wax."
Through the roof,<b>Meaning:</b> Increasing to a very high level. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> A metaphor for something rising so fast it breaks through the top of a building. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> Gas prices have gone through the roof this month.
Throw a curveball,<b>Meaning:</b> To surprise someone with something difficult or unexpected. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> From baseball; a pitch that moves in an unexpected direction. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> The interviewer threw me a curveball by asking about my childhood.
Thumb your nose at,<b>Meaning:</b> To show lack of respect or defiance. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> A physical gesture of placing a thumb on the nose and wiggling the fingers. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> He thumbed his nose at the company rules and wore jeans to the meeting.
Tie one's hands,"<b>Meaning:</b> To prevent someone from acting as they wish. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> A metaphor for being physically bound. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> I want to help you, but the regulations tie my hands."
Tied to someone's apron strings,<b>Meaning:</b> Being too dependent on or controlled by someone (usually a mother). <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> From the days when children were literally tied to their mothers' aprons for safety. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> He’s 30 years old and still tied to his mother's apron strings.
Time is money,"<b>Meaning:</b> Time is a valuable resource, so don't waste it. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Attributed to Benjamin Franklin in 1748. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> We need to finish this meeting quickly; time is money."
Tip of the tongue,"<b>Meaning:</b> When you almost remember a word but cannot quite say it. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> A physical sensation that the word is right at the edge of the mouth. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> Her name is on the tip of my tongue, but I just can't remember it!"
To a T,"<b>Meaning:</b> Perfectly; exactly. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Possibly from 'T-square,' a tool used for precision in drawing. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> This new job suits me to a T."
Too close for comfort,<b>Meaning:</b> So close that it makes you feel nervous or worried. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> A metaphor for danger being physically near. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> That lightning strike was too close for comfort.
"Too little, too late","<b>Meaning:</b> Not enough help, and it arrives after the problem has already happened. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> A 19th-century military and political phrase. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> They offered a refund after I sued them; it was too little, too late."
Top dog,<b>Meaning:</b> The most important or powerful person in a group. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> From dog fighting; the winner was the 'top dog' while the loser was the 'underdog.' <br><br> <b>Example:</b> He’s the top dog at the law firm.
Tough act to follow,<b>Meaning:</b> Someone or something that was so good it will be hard for the next person to be as successful. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> From vaudeville or theater. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> The previous manager was loved by everyone; he's a tough act to follow.
Touch base,<b>Meaning:</b> To briefly contact someone to update them or check on progress. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> From baseball; a player briefly touching a base. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> Let's touch base next week to see how the project is going.
Turn the tables,"<b>Meaning:</b> To reverse a situation so that you now have the advantage. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> From board games like backgammon, where turning the board would change the players' positions. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> She used to be the boss, but now the tables have turned and I'm her manager."
Two heads are better than one,<b>Meaning:</b> Two people working together can solve a problem more easily than one. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> A proverb from at least the 16th century. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> Come help me with this puzzle; two heads are better than one.
Two shakes of a lamb's tail,<b>Meaning:</b> Very quickly; in a very short time. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> An observation that lambs shake their tails very fast. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> I'll be there in two shakes of a lamb's tail.
Under your nose,"<b>Meaning:</b> Right in front of you, but you fail to see it. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> A metaphor for something being so close it is effectively invisible. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> I was looking for my glasses, and they were right under my nose on the table."
Until you are blue in the face,"<b>Meaning:</b> To do something for a long time without success. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Gasping for breath (turning blue) from talking too much. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> You can argue until you are blue in the face, but I’m not going."
Up the ante,"<b>Meaning:</b> To increase the costs, risks, or stakes of a situation. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> From poker; the 'ante' is the bet players must make before the game starts. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> The competition lowered their prices, so we had to up the ante with a new marketing campaign."
Uphill battle,<b>Meaning:</b> A very difficult task that requires a lot of effort over a long time. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> A military metaphor; it is much harder to fight while moving up a hill. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> Getting the government to change the law will be an uphill battle.
Value for money,"<b>Meaning:</b> Something that is worth the price you paid for it. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> A standard consumer phrase. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> This laptop was expensive, but it's great value for money."
Walking on air,"<b>Meaning:</b> Feeling extremely happy and excited. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> A metaphor for feeling so light with joy that you don't touch the ground. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> After he proposed, she was walking on air."
Warts and all,"<b>Meaning:</b> Including all the bad or unpleasant features of something; not just the good parts. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Oliver Cromwell demanded his portrait be painted accurately, including his warts. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> I love him for who he is, warts and all."
Wash your dirty linen in public,<b>Meaning:</b> To discuss private or embarrassing matters in front of others. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Comparing private family secrets to dirty clothes that should be washed at home. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> Please don't argue about our finances in front of our friends; don't wash your dirty linen in public.
Watch your step,"<b>Meaning:</b> To be careful about what you say or do to avoid trouble. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> A literal warning to avoid tripping, used metaphorically. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> The boss is in a bad mood, so watch your step today."
Water under the bridge,"<b>Meaning:</b> Past events that cannot be changed and are no longer worth worrying about. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Water that has already flowed past is gone forever. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> We fought years ago, but that's water under the bridge now."
Wear the pants,<b>Meaning:</b> To be the person who makes the decisions in a relationship or household. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> From a time when only men wore trousers (pants). <br><br> <b>Example:</b> It’s clear that Sarah is the one who wears the pants in that family.
Well-heeled,<b>Meaning:</b> Wealthy. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Likely from the 16th century; having good shoes (with solid heels) was a sign of being rich. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> The restaurant was full of well-heeled businessmen.
Wet blanket,<b>Meaning:</b> A person who spoils other people's fun. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> From the practice of using wet blankets to put out fires. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> Don't be such a wet blanket; come to the party with us!
What's the catch?,<b>Meaning:</b> What is the hidden drawback or hidden cost in an offer that seems too good? <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> From fishing; the 'catch' is the hook hidden inside the bait. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> You’re giving away free phones? What’s the catch?
When the chips are down,"<b>Meaning:</b> In a difficult or critical situation when a final decision must be made. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> From gambling; when all the betting chips have been placed on the table. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> When the chips are down, you find out who your real friends are."
Whet your appetite,<b>Meaning:</b> To increase your desire for something. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> 'Whet' means to sharpen (like a knife). <br><br> <b>Example:</b> The trailer for the movie really whetted my appetite.
Whistle-stop tour,<b>Meaning:</b> A very fast trip where you visit many places in a short time. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> From politicians traveling by train and stopping briefly at small stations (whistle-stops). <br><br> <b>Example:</b> We’re doing a whistle-stop tour of Europe—five cities in seven days.
Wild horse couldn't drag me,<b>Meaning:</b> Nothing could persuade me to do something. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> An exaggeration of physical force. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> Wild horses couldn't drag me back to that restaurant; the food was terrible.
With flying colors,<b>Meaning:</b> To succeed easily and impressively. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Ships returning from a victorious battle would fly all their flags (colors). <br><br> <b>Example:</b> She passed her bar exam with flying colors.
Wolf in sheep's clothing,"<b>Meaning:</b> A dangerous person pretending to be harmless. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> From the Bible and Aesop's fables. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> He seems helpful, but he's a wolf in sheep's clothing."
Word for word,<b>Meaning:</b> Exactly as it was originally said or written. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> A literal description of accuracy. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> I repeated the conversation word for word.
Work your fingers to the bone,<b>Meaning:</b> To work extremely hard. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> An exaggeration of wearing away skin and flesh through hard labor. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> She worked her fingers to the bone to put her children through college.
Worth its weight in gold,<b>Meaning:</b> Extremely valuable or useful. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> A literal comparison to the price of gold. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> This old truck is worth its weight in gold on a farm.
Wrap your head around,<b>Meaning:</b> To understand something complicated. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> A metaphor for the mind 'encompassing' a difficult idea. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> I’m trying to wrap my head around this new tax law.
Wreak havoc,"<b>Meaning:</b> To cause a lot of damage, confusion, or trouble. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> 'Havoc' was an old military command for soldiers to start looting a city. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> The storm wreaked havoc on the coastal towns."
Writing's on the wall,"<b>Meaning:</b> It is clear that something bad is going to happen. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> From the Bible (Book of Daniel); a mysterious hand wrote a message of doom on the king's wall. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> After three months of losses, the writing's on the wall for the company."
You bet!,<b>Meaning:</b> Certainly; you can be sure of it. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> From gambling; implying that the outcome is so certain you would bet money on it. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> 'Are you coming to the party?' 'You bet!'
"You can lead a horse to water, but you can't make it drink","<b>Meaning:</b> You can give someone an opportunity, but you can't force them to take it. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> A proverb from the 12th century. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> I gave him all the books he needs to study, but he won't open them; you can lead a horse to water..."
Young at heart,"<b>Meaning:</b> Having a youthful spirit despite being old. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> A metaphor for the heart retaining its energy and joy. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> My grandmother is 85, but she’s still very young at heart."
Your heart is in the right place,"<b>Meaning:</b> You have good intentions even if you made a mistake. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> A metaphor for the source of motivation being moral and kind. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> He broke the vase while trying to clean it, but his heart was in the right place."
Zero in on,<b>Meaning:</b> To focus all your attention on one specific thing. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> From military targeting; adjusting a weapon's sights to hit the exact center. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> We need to zero in on the cause of the technical error.
A penny saved is a penny earned,<b>Meaning:</b> It is as useful to save money that you already have as it is to earn more. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Often attributed to Benjamin Franklin. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> I'm going to cook at home tonight; a penny saved is a penny earned.
Ace in the hole,"<b>Meaning:</b> A secret advantage kept in reserve until it is needed. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> From stud poker, where one card is dealt face down (in the 'hole'). <br><br> <b>Example:</b> The witness's testimony was the lawyer's ace in the hole."
Against the clock,<b>Meaning:</b> Doing something as fast as possible to finish before a deadline. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> A metaphor for racing against time itself. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> We were working against the clock to finish the presentation.
Air your dirty laundry,"<b>Meaning:</b> To discuss private or scandalous information in public. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Same as 'wash your dirty linen.' <br><br> <b>Example:</b> They started arguing about their divorce in the middle of the restaurant, airing all their dirty laundry."
All ears,<b>Meaning:</b> Fully focused and listening carefully. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> A metaphor for the entire body becoming a listening organ. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> Tell me about your new job; I'm all ears.
All eyes on me,"<b>Meaning:</b> Everyone is watching me. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Standard descriptive phrase. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> When I walked onto the stage, it felt like all eyes were on me."
All thumbs,"<b>Meaning:</b> Very clumsy, especially with your hands. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> If all fingers were thumbs, it would be impossible to do delicate work. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> I'm all thumbs today; I've dropped my phone three times."
An arm and a leg,<b>Meaning:</b> A very large amount of money. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> See 'Cost an arm and a leg.' <br><br> <b>Example:</b> These shoes cost an arm and a leg.
As a rule,"<b>Meaning:</b> Usually; most of the time. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Based on a general principle or habit. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> As a rule, I don't eat after 8 PM."
At each other's throats,<b>Meaning:</b> Arguing or fighting fiercely. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> A metaphor for a physical fight where people grab each other by the neck. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> The two politicians have been at each other's throats for years.
At the end of your rope,<b>Meaning:</b> Having no more patience or energy left to deal with a situation. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> A metaphor for a tethered animal that has reached the furthest limit of its rope. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> The baby has been crying for hours; I'm at the end of my rope.
Back on your feet,"<b>Meaning:</b> Healthy or successful again after a period of illness or failure. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> A metaphor for standing up after falling down. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> It took a few months, but he's finally back on his feet after the surgery."
Back to basics,<b>Meaning:</b> Returning to the fundamental or most important parts of something. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> A common phrase in education and business. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> The product is too complicated; we need to go back to basics.
Bad blood,<b>Meaning:</b> Feelings of hate or resentment between people because of past arguments. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Ancient belief that emotions changed the literal physical quality of the blood. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> There is a lot of bad blood between the two families.
Ballpark figure,"<b>Meaning:</b> A rough estimate or an approximate number. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> From baseball; being inside the 'ballpark' means being close to the target even if not exact. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> I don't need the exact price, just give me a ballpark figure."
Barking up the wrong tree,"<b>Meaning:</b> Following a wrong line of thought or accusing the wrong person. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Hunting dogs barking at a tree where they think a raccoon is hiding. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> If you think I took your lunch, you're barking up the wrong tree."
Bated breath,<b>Meaning:</b> Feeling very anxious or excited while waiting for something. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> From Shakespeare; 'bated' means 'abated' or held. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> We waited with bated breath for the exam results.
Beating a dead horse,<b>Meaning:</b> Wasting time on something that is already finished or cannot be changed. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Whipping a dead horse to make it run is pointless. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> We've already decided to cancel the project; talking about it now is just beating a dead horse.
Behind the scenes,<b>Meaning:</b> Done privately or secretly; away from public view. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> From theater; the work that happens backstage where the audience cannot see it. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> A lot of work goes on behind the scenes to make the festival a success.
Bend over backwards,<b>Meaning:</b> To try extremely hard to please or help someone. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> From gymnastics; a difficult physical move performed to show skill or effort. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> I bent over backwards to finish the report on time for her.
Big cheese,"<b>Meaning:</b> An important and powerful person in an organization. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Likely from the Urdu word 'chiz' (thing), adapted by British colonial officials. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> He’s the big cheese at the bank."
Bite the bullet,"<b>Meaning:</b> To force yourself to do something unpleasant or difficult. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Before anesthesia, soldiers would bite on a lead bullet to endure the pain of surgery. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> I don't want to go to the dentist, but I just have to bite the bullet."
Blow the whistle,<b>Meaning:</b> To report a person or organization for doing something wrong. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> See 'Whistle-blower.' <br><br> <b>Example:</b> He blew the whistle on the company's illegal waste disposal.
Break a sweat,<b>Meaning:</b> To exert a lot of physical or mental effort. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> A literal description of physical exercise. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> He’s so good at math he can solve these problems without even breaking a sweat.
Front,Back
A bird in the hand is worth two in the bush,"<b>Meaning:</b> It is better to keep what you have than to risk losing it by trying to get something better. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Medieval falconry; having a bird caught was better than seeing two others in the wild. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> I might get a better job offer later, but I'm taking this one; a bird in the hand is worth two in the bush."
A leopard can't change its spots,"<b>Meaning:</b> People cannot change their basic nature or character. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> From the Bible (Jeremiah 13:23). <br><br> <b>Example:</b> He promised to stop lying, but he did it again; a leopard can't change its spots."
A picture paints a thousand words,<b>Meaning:</b> A visual image can convey complex ideas more effectively than a description. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Coined in the early 20th century by advertising executives. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> Just look at this photo of the damage; a picture paints a thousand words.
A sight for sore eyes,"<b>Meaning:</b> Someone or something that you are very happy to see. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Suggests that looking at the person is so pleasant it 'heals' tired eyes. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> After a week in the desert, that oasis was a sight for sore eyes."
All in the same boat,"<b>Meaning:</b> Being in the same difficult circumstances as others. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> If a small boat sinks, everyone on board faces the same fate regardless of status. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> We're all losing money during this recession, so we're all in the same boat."
An axe to grind,<b>Meaning:</b> To have a strong personal opinion or a selfish reason for being involved in something. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Attributed to Benjamin Franklin; a man tricked a boy into sharpening his axe for him. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> I don't trust his criticism of the company; he has an axe to grind because they fired him.
Back to the drawing board,"<b>Meaning:</b> To start over because a previous attempt failed. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> From a WWII cartoon about a crashed plane; the designer needs to go back to his sketches. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> The prototype exploded, so it's back to the drawing board for us."
Beggars can't be choosers,"<b>Meaning:</b> People who are in need must accept whatever help is offered to them. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> A proverb dating back to the 1500s. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> The apartment is small, but it's the only one I can afford; beggars can't be choosers."
Between the devil and the deep blue sea,"<b>Meaning:</b> Facing two equally dangerous or unpleasant situations. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Nautical; the 'devil' was a seam on the ship's hull that was difficult to reach without falling into the sea. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> If I stay, I'm bored; if I leave, I'm broke. I'm between the devil and the deep blue sea."
Bite your tongue,"<b>Meaning:</b> To make a conscious effort to stop yourself from saying something. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> A physical metaphor for holding back speech. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> I wanted to tell him he was wrong, but I had to bite my tongue."
Blind leading the blind,<b>Meaning:</b> A situation where people who know nothing are being led by someone who also knows nothing. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> From the Bible (Matthew 15:14). <br><br> <b>Example:</b> Neither of us knows how to fix a car; it's the blind leading the blind.
Blow off steam,<b>Meaning:</b> To do something to get rid of stress or strong emotions. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> From steam engines; letting off excess pressure to prevent an explosion. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> I went for a long run to blow off steam after the argument.
Born with a silver spoon in one's mouth,"<b>Meaning:</b> Born into a very wealthy and privileged family. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Traditionally, godparents gave silver spoons to babies at christenings; only the wealthy could afford them. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> He doesn't understand the cost of living because he was born with a silver spoon in his mouth."
Bring home the bacon,"<b>Meaning:</b> To earn the money that a family needs to live. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Possibly from an old English tradition where a couple who didn't argue for a year won a side of bacon. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> Now that she has a high-paying job, she's the one bringing home the bacon."
Bull in a china shop,"<b>Meaning:</b> A person who is very clumsy or careless in a delicate situation. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> A literal image of a large, powerful animal in a store full of fragile porcelain. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> Don't let him handle the negotiations; he's like a bull in a china shop."
Burn the candle at both ends,<b>Meaning:</b> To work too hard without getting enough rest. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> A candle lit at both ends burns out twice as fast. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> He’s been staying late and waking up early; he's burning the candle at both ends.
Bury the hatchet,"<b>Meaning:</b> To stop fighting and make peace. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> From a Native American custom of literally burying weapons to symbolize the end of a conflict. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> After years of arguing, the two brothers finally decided to bury the hatchet."
Butterflies in one's stomach,<b>Meaning:</b> Feeling very nervous or excited. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Describes the fluttery physical sensation in the abdomen caused by adrenaline. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> I always get butterflies in my stomach before I go on stage.
By the book,"<b>Meaning:</b> Following rules or procedures exactly. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Refers to following a manual, the Bible, or a law book. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> The inspector does everything by the book, so make sure your paperwork is perfect."
Call the shots,"<b>Meaning:</b> To be the person who makes the important decisions. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Likely from target practice or billiards, where the player announces which 'shot' they will make. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> In this office, the manager is the one who calls the shots."
Can't see the forest for the trees,<b>Meaning:</b> Being so involved in the small details that you miss the big picture. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Focusing on individual trees prevents you from seeing the entire forest. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> He's so worried about the font size that he can't see the forest for the trees regarding the whole project.
Carry the torch,"<b>Meaning:</b> To continue a cause or tradition, or to remain in love with someone who doesn't love you back. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> From ancient Greek relay races where a torch was passed to keep the flame alive. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> Even after they broke up, he continued to carry the torch for her."
Cast pearls before swine,<b>Meaning:</b> To offer something valuable to people who do not appreciate it. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> From the Bible (Matthew 7:6). <br><br> <b>Example:</b> Explaining classical music to them is like casting pearls before swine.
Change of heart,"<b>Meaning:</b> A reversal of one's feelings or opinion. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> The heart is seen as the seat of emotions and decisions. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> I was going to sell the house, but I had a change of heart."
Chew the fat,"<b>Meaning:</b> To have a long, casual chat. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Possibly from sailors chewing on tough salt pork while talking. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> We sat on the porch for hours just chewing the fat."
Clam up,"<b>Meaning:</b> To suddenly stop talking, usually due to shyness or fear. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> A clam tightly shuts its shell when it feels threatened. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> As soon as the police arrived, the witness clammed up."
Clear the air,<b>Meaning:</b> To resolve a tense situation by talking openly about it. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Likens an argument to a heavy atmosphere that is 'cleared' by a thunderstorm. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> We need to have a meeting to clear the air about these recent rumors.
Close but no cigar,<b>Meaning:</b> Coming near to success but ultimately failing. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> From fairground games where cigars were given as prizes for winning. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> You almost guessed the right number—close but no cigar.
Come clean,<b>Meaning:</b> To tell the whole truth about something you have been hiding. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> A metaphor for washing away the 'dirt' of a lie. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> It’s time for you to come clean about where you were last night.
Cost an arm and a leg,"<b>Meaning:</b> To be extremely expensive. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Possibly from the high cost of portraits that included limbs, or the value of one's own body parts. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> That new car cost me an arm and a leg."
Couch potato,"<b>Meaning:</b> A person who spends a lot of time sitting or lying down, usually watching TV. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Coined in the 1970s; compares a person to a vegetable that just sits there. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> Stop being such a couch potato and go outside!"
Cut to the quick,"<b>Meaning:</b> To deeply hurt someone's feelings. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> The 'quick' is the living, sensitive flesh under the skin or nails. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> His cold remark about her work cut her to the quick."
Dead ringer,<b>Meaning:</b> Someone or something that looks exactly like another. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> From horse racing; a 'ringer' was a fast horse substituted for a slow one. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> That guy is a dead ringer for my brother.
Don't give up your day job,<b>Meaning:</b> A humorous way to tell someone they are not very good at something. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Implies that their hobby will never earn them enough money to quit their main job. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> You want to be a singer? Don't give up your day job.
Double-edged sword,"<b>Meaning:</b> Something that has both favorable and unfavorable consequences. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> A sword with two sharp edges can hurt the person using it as well as the enemy. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> Fame is a double-edged sword; you get money, but you lose your privacy."
Down to the wire,<b>Meaning:</b> Something that is not decided until the very last moment. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> From horse racing; a wire was stretched across the finish line to help identify the winner. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> The election is going right down to the wire.
Draw a blank,"<b>Meaning:</b> To be unable to remember something or get a response. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> From old lotteries where some tickets were blank and won nothing. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> I tried to remember his name, but I drew a blank."
Drop a bombshell,<b>Meaning:</b> To announce shocking or unexpected news. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Compares the impact of news to the explosion of a bomb. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> She dropped a bombshell when she announced she was moving to France tomorrow.
Eager beaver,"<b>Meaning:</b> A person who is very hardworking and enthusiastic. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Beavers are known for their constant activity in building dams. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> The new intern is a real eager beaver, always asking for more work."
Eat your heart out,<b>Meaning:</b> To feel great jealousy or regret. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Ancient belief that grief or envy physically 'consumed' the heart. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> I just won a free trip to Hawaii—eat your heart out!
Elbow grease,"<b>Meaning:</b> Hard physical work or effort. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> A humorous way of saying the only 'oil' needed to fix something is the sweat from your own elbows. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> The table is dirty, but it'll look fine with a little elbow grease."
Face value,<b>Meaning:</b> Accepting something exactly as it appears without looking for a deeper meaning. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> From the value printed on the 'face' of a coin or banknote. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> Don't take his promises at face value; he often breaks them.
"Fall for hook, line, and sinker","<b>Meaning:</b> To be completely deceived by someone. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> From fishing; a fish that swallows the hook, the fishing line, and the lead weight (sinker) is totally caught. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> He told me he was a millionaire, and I fell for it hook, line, and sinker."
Far cry from,<b>Meaning:</b> Very different from something else. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Refers to a distance so great that a shout (cry) cannot be heard. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> This cheap hotel is a far cry from the luxury resort we stayed in last year.
Feather one's own nest,<b>Meaning:</b> To make yourself rich by taking unfair advantage of a situation. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Birds use soft feathers to make their nests comfortable. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> The politician was accused of using public funds to feather his own nest.
Flash in the pan,"<b>Meaning:</b> Something that starts out well but doesn't last or succeed. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> From old muskets; sometimes the gunpowder in the 'pan' would flare up without actually firing the bullet. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> His first book was a bestseller, but his others failed; he was just a flash in the pan."
Fly off the handle,<b>Meaning:</b> To lose one's temper suddenly and unexpectedly. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Like an axe head that flies off the wooden handle because it is loose and dangerous. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> He tends to fly off the handle if you criticize his driving.
Follow in someone's footsteps,<b>Meaning:</b> To do the same job or live in the same way as someone else before you. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> A literal metaphor for walking in the path made by a predecessor. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> She followed in her mother's footsteps and became a doctor.
Food for thought,<b>Meaning:</b> Something worth thinking about carefully. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Likens information to food that the mind needs to 'digest.' <br><br> <b>Example:</b> Your suggestion about changing the schedule gave me some food for thought.
Fool's gold,"<b>Meaning:</b> Something that looks valuable but is actually worthless. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Refers to the mineral pyrite, which looks like gold but has no value. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> That 'get rich quick' scheme turned out to be fool's gold."
For good measure,<b>Meaning:</b> Doing something extra to make sure a result is achieved or to be generous. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> From merchants adding a little extra to a weighed amount to ensure the customer was satisfied. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> I cleaned the kitchen and mopped the floors for good measure.
Get cold feet,"<b>Meaning:</b> To become nervous and pull out of a deal or event at the last minute. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Likely from the 19th century; having 'cold feet' meant you couldn't stand your ground in a fight. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> He was going to propose, but he got cold feet and didn't do it."
Get the sack,"<b>Meaning:</b> To be fired from a job. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> In the past, tradesmen carried their tools in a sack; when they were fired, they were given their sack back to leave. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> He got the sack for being late every day."
Get up on the wrong side of the bed,<b>Meaning:</b> To be grumpy or in a bad mood all day. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Ancient Roman superstition that exiting the bed on the left side was bad luck. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> Don't talk to him today; he got up on the wrong side of the bed.
Give someone a run for their money,<b>Meaning:</b> To be a strong competitor and make it difficult for someone else to win. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> From horse racing; ensuring that the person who bet on you gets a good performance for their stake. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> Our small team gave the champions a run for their money.
Go against the grain,<b>Meaning:</b> To do something that is contrary to your natural character or common sense. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Wood is easier to cut with the 'grain'; cutting against it is difficult and causes splinters. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> It goes against the grain for me to lie to my parents.
Go back on your word,"<b>Meaning:</b> To fail to do what you promised. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> A metaphor for physically turning around and leaving a promise behind. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> He said he would help me move, but he went back on his word."
Green with envy,<b>Meaning:</b> Extremely jealous. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Green has been associated with illness and bile since the time of the Greeks. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> She was green with envy when she saw my new car.
Grin and bear it,"<b>Meaning:</b> To accept a difficult or unpleasant situation without complaining. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> From the 18th century; 'grin' used to mean showing teeth in pain, not just happiness. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> There’s no point in complaining about the rain; we just have to grin and bear it."
Hair of the dog,<b>Meaning:</b> A small amount of alcohol taken to ease a hangover. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> From the old belief that you could cure a dog bite by applying a hair from the dog that bit you. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> I feel terrible this morning; I need a hair of the dog.
Hand to mouth,"<b>Meaning:</b> Having only just enough money or food to survive. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> A literal description of eating everything you earn immediately with nothing left over. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> When I was a student, I lived hand to mouth."
Hang in there,"<b>Meaning:</b> Don't give up; stay patient during a difficult time. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Likely from the 'Hang in there, baby' kitten posters of the 1970s. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> I know the work is hard, but hang in there—the weekend is coming."
Have a sweet tooth,<b>Meaning:</b> To have a strong liking for sweet foods like candy or cake. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> From the 14th century; 'tooth' was used to mean 'appetite.' <br><br> <b>Example:</b> I can't say no to chocolate; I have a real sweet tooth.
Head over heels,<b>Meaning:</b> Deeply in love. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Originally 'heels over head' (like a somersault); eventually inverted. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> He’s head over heels in love with his new girlfriend.
Hit the hay,<b>Meaning:</b> To go to sleep. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> From the days when mattresses were sacks filled with hay. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> I’m exhausted; it’s time for me to hit the hay.
In the same breath,<b>Meaning:</b> Saying two contradictory things almost at the same time. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> A literal description of talking so fast the points are in one 'breath.' <br><br> <b>Example:</b> He said he loved the car but in the same breath complained it was too small.
It takes two to tango,"<b>Meaning:</b> Both people involved in a situation or argument are responsible for it. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> The tango is a dance that cannot be performed alone. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> She says it's all his fault, but it takes two to tango."
Keep a straight face,"<b>Meaning:</b> To stop yourself from laughing. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Maintaining a neutral facial expression despite finding something funny. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> I tried to keep a straight face when he tripped, but I couldn't."
Keep your eyes peeled,<b>Meaning:</b> To watch very carefully for something. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Like 'peeling' away an eyelid to make sure nothing is missed. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> Keep your eyes peeled for the highway exit.
Kill someone with kindness,"<b>Meaning:</b> To be excessively kind to someone, especially someone who is being mean to you. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Used by Shakespeare; the idea that kindness can be overwhelming or disarming. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> Instead of arguing with the rude customer, she killed him with kindness."
Last but not least,"<b>Meaning:</b> The last person mentioned is just as important as the ones before. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> A standard rhetorical device used in speeches since the 1500s. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> And last but not least, I'd like to thank my wife."
Leave no stone unturned,<b>Meaning:</b> To try every possible way to find something or solve a problem. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> From an ancient Greek legend about a general looking for treasure. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> The police promised to leave no stone unturned in their investigation.
Let sleeping dogs lie,"<b>Meaning:</b> To leave a situation alone to avoid causing trouble. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> A dog that is asleep is peaceful; waking it might cause it to bite. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> I know he was rude yesterday, but let’s just let sleeping dogs lie."
Like a broken record,<b>Meaning:</b> Someone who repeats the same thing over and over. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> From vinyl records; a scratch would cause the needle to jump back and play the same part repeatedly. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> My dad is like a broken record when it comes to saving money.
Long shot,"<b>Meaning:</b> An attempt that has a very low chance of succeeding. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> From shooting; a target that is very far away is very hard to hit. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> It’s a long shot, but I’m going to apply for the CEO position."
Look before you leap,<b>Meaning:</b> Think about the consequences before you act. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> From Aesop's fables; a fox tells a goat to check the depth of a well before jumping in. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> You should read the contract carefully; look before you leap.
Lose your marbles,"<b>Meaning:</b> To lose your mind or go crazy. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> From the 19th century; 'marbles' was slang for one's wits or intelligence. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> I think he's lost his marbles, talking to the bushes like that."
Make a killing,<b>Meaning:</b> To earn a large amount of money very quickly. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> From hunting; a single 'kill' provides a massive amount of food for the effort. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> He made a killing on the stock market last year.
Make waves,"<b>Meaning:</b> To cause trouble or disturb a stable situation. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> A boat moving through calm water creates waves that can upset other smaller boats. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> She’s new at the job, so she doesn't want to make waves by suggesting changes."
Money talks,<b>Meaning:</b> People with money have a lot of influence and power. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> A 17th-century proverb; wealth is more persuasive than words. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> He got the best table in the restaurant because money talks.
My way or the highway,"<b>Meaning:</b> You must do things exactly as I say, or you can leave. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> A modern American idiom expressing a refusal to compromise. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> The new boss told us it's his way or the highway."
Neck and neck,<b>Meaning:</b> Very close together in a race or competition. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> From horse racing; when two horses are so close their necks are side-by-side. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> The two candidates are neck and neck in the polls.
No-brainer,<b>Meaning:</b> A decision that is so easy it requires no thought. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Modern slang; the task is so simple a brain isn't even needed. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> Choosing the job with better pay was a no-brainer.
Not my cup of tea,"<b>Meaning:</b> Not something that I like or am interested in. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> From the British culture of tea-drinking; some blends are simply not to one's taste. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> I know everyone loves that show, but it’s just not my cup of tea."
Off the top of my head,"<b>Meaning:</b> From memory, without looking it up or thinking deeply. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Metaphor for information sitting right on the 'surface' of the mind. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> I don't know the exact price off the top of my head, but it's around $50."
Once in a blue moon,"<b>Meaning:</b> Very rarely. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> A 'blue moon' is the rare second full moon in a single month. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> My brother lives in Japan, so I only see him once in a blue moon."
Out of the blue,"<b>Meaning:</b> Suddenly and unexpectedly. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Short for 'a bolt from the blue,' like a lightning strike from a clear blue sky. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> She called me out of the blue to offer me a job."
Over the top,"<b>Meaning:</b> Excessive, exaggerated, or unnecessary. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> From WWI; soldiers climbing out of their trenches (over the top) into extreme danger. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> The decorations for the birthday party were a bit over the top."
Penny for your thoughts,"<b>Meaning:</b> A way of asking someone what they are thinking about. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> From the 16th century, when a penny was a reasonable price for someone's attention. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> You’ve been staring out the window for ten minutes; a penny for your thoughts?"
Play it by ear,"<b>Meaning:</b> To decide how to deal with a situation as it develops, rather than having a plan. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> From musicians who can play a song just by hearing it, without sheet music. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> We don't have a schedule for the trip; we'll just play it by ear."
Pull out all the stops,<b>Meaning:</b> To use all your energy and resources to achieve something. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> From pipe organs; 'stops' control airflow. Pulling them all out makes the loudest sound possible. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> They pulled out all the stops for their daughter's wedding.
Put all your eggs in one basket,"<b>Meaning:</b> To depend entirely on one plan or person. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> A literal warning; if the basket drops, all the eggs break. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> Don't invest all your money in one company; don't put all your eggs in one basket."
Put your foot in your mouth,"<b>Meaning:</b> To say something embarrassing or tactless. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> A metaphor for 'tripping' over your own words. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> I really put my foot in my mouth when I asked her about her husband, forgetting they had divorced."
Raining cats and dogs,<b>Meaning:</b> Raining very heavily. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Uncertain; possibly from straw-roofed houses where animals would hide and get washed off during heavy rain. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> We can't go to the beach today; it's raining cats and dogs.
Read between the lines,"<b>Meaning:</b> To find the hidden meaning in something. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> From military codes where real messages were written in invisible ink between the lines of a normal letter. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> He said he was 'fine,' but if you read between the lines, you can tell he's upset."
Rule of thumb,"<b>Meaning:</b> A broad, general rule based on experience rather than exact measurement. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Using the thumb as a rough unit of measurement (roughly an inch). <br><br> <b>Example:</b> As a rule of thumb, always arrive 10 minutes early for an interview."
See eye to eye,<b>Meaning:</b> To agree with someone. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> From the Bible (Isaiah 52:8). <br><br> <b>Example:</b> My father and I don't always see eye to eye on politics.
Sit tight,<b>Meaning:</b> To wait patiently and not take any action. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> To stay in one place firmly. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> Sit tight; the doctor will be with you in a moment.
Skeleton in the closet,<b>Meaning:</b> An embarrassing or scandalous secret. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Refers to hiding a 'dead' secret where no one will find it. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> Every family has a skeleton in the closet.
Sleep on it,<b>Meaning:</b> To wait until the next day to make a decision. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> The belief that a night's rest gives a person a clearer perspective. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> That’s a big decision; why don't you sleep on it and tell me tomorrow?
Under one's thumb,<b>Meaning:</b> Under someone's total control or influence. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> A metaphor for being physically held down by someone's thumb. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> He’s been under his manager's thumb for years and never makes his own decisions.
Under the counter,"<b>Meaning:</b> Sold or done secretly and often illegally. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Refers to goods hidden beneath a shop counter to avoid taxes or regulations. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> During the shortage, some shops were selling bread under the counter."
Under the table,"<b>Meaning:</b> Done secretly, usually involving a bribe or illegal payment. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Passing money beneath a table so that others (or the government) cannot see the transaction. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> He was paid under the table to avoid paying income tax."
Up a creek without a paddle,"<b>Meaning:</b> In a very difficult situation with no way to fix it. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Nautical; being in a boat in a current with no means of steering or propulsion. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> If the car breaks down now, we'll be up a creek without a paddle."
Up for grabs,<b>Meaning:</b> Available for anyone to take or win. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Likely from games or markets where items were thrown into the air for people to 'grab.' <br><br> <b>Example:</b> There are several shifts up for grabs if anyone wants extra hours.
Up in arms,<b>Meaning:</b> Very angry and ready to protest or fight. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> 'Arms' refers to weapons; historically meant a population was literally picking up guns to rebel. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> The neighbors are up in arms about the new construction project.
Up to scratch,"<b>Meaning:</b> Reaching an acceptable standard. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> From bare-knuckle boxing, where a fighter had to walk to a 'scratch' line in the center of the ring to prove they could still fight. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> Your recent work hasn't been up to scratch lately."
Upset the applecart,<b>Meaning:</b> To spoil a plan or disturb the status quo. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> From the 18th century; knocking over a cart full of apples ruins the vendor's organized display and business. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> We have a good system going; don't upset the applecart by changing the rules now.
Vanish into thin air,"<b>Meaning:</b> To disappear completely and suddenly. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Used by Shakespeare in The Tempest; the idea of something solid turning into invisible gas. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> I left my keys on the table, but they seem to have vanished into thin air."
Variety is the spice of life,<b>Meaning:</b> New and different experiences make life more interesting. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> From a 1785 poem by William Cowper. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> I like to travel to a different country every year because variety is the spice of life.
Wait for the other shoe to drop,"<b>Meaning:</b> To wait for the next (usually bad) thing to happen in a series of events. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> From living in thin-walled apartments; you hear a neighbor drop one shoe to the floor and wait for the sound of the second. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> The company cut salaries, and now we're just waiting for the other shoe to drop—layoffs."
Wake up on the wrong side of the bed,<b>Meaning:</b> To be in a bad mood from the moment you wake up. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> From an ancient Roman superstition that it was bad luck to step out of bed with the left foot. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> He's been grumpy all day; he must have woken up on the wrong side of the bed.
Walk on eggshells,<b>Meaning:</b> To be extremely careful not to upset or offend someone. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> A metaphor for treading so lightly that you wouldn't even break a fragile eggshell. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> Everyone in the office is walking on eggshells since the boss got angry.
Wash your hands of something,"<b>Meaning:</b> To refuse to be responsible for or involved in something anymore. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> From the Bible; Pontius Pilate literally washed his hands to show he wasn't responsible for the execution of Jesus. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> I've tried to help him, but now I'm washing my hands of the whole situation."
Watch someone like a hawk,<b>Meaning:</b> To watch someone very closely and suspiciously. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Hawks are known for their incredibly sharp eyesight and intense focus while hunting. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> The security guard watched the suspicious man like a hawk.
Wear many hats,"<b>Meaning:</b> To have many different roles or responsibilities. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Hats were traditionally associated with specific jobs (e.g., a chef’s hat, a police cap). <br><br> <b>Example:</b> In a small startup, you often have to wear many hats."
Wear your heart on your sleeve,<b>Meaning:</b> To show your emotions openly rather than hiding them. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> From medieval jousting; knights would wear a lady's scarf or colors on their sleeve to show their devotion. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> You always know when she's sad because she wears her heart on her sleeve.
Weather the storm,"<b>Meaning:</b> To survive a difficult period or situation. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> A nautical term for a ship surviving a literal storm at sea. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> The company is losing money, but we hope to weather the storm until next year."
Wet behind the ears,"<b>Meaning:</b> Young, inexperienced, or naive. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Refers to a newborn animal that is still wet from birth, with the area behind the ears being the last to dry. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> He’s a good kid, but he’s still a bit wet behind the ears when it comes to business."
What goes around comes around,"<b>Meaning:</b> A person's actions (good or bad) will eventually have consequences for them. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> A modern proverb related to the concept of karma. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> He was mean to his coworkers, and now no one wants to help him; what goes around comes around."
"When in Rome, do as the Romans do","<b>Meaning:</b> When visiting a new place, follow the customs of the people who live there. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Attributed to St. Ambrose in the 4th century. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> I don't usually eat snails, but when in Rome, do as the Romans do."
"When it rains, it pours","<b>Meaning:</b> When one bad thing happens, several other bad things happen at the same time. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> From a 1911 advertising slogan for Morton Salt, which didn't clump even in the rain. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> First I lost my keys, then my car wouldn't start—when it rains, it pours."
When pigs fly,"<b>Meaning:</b> Something that will never happen. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> A humorous image of an animal that is physically incapable of flight. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> 'Do you think he'll apologize?' 'Yeah, when pigs fly!'"
Whistle-blower,<b>Meaning:</b> A person who reveals secret information about a person or organization doing something wrong. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> From referees or police using a whistle to stop play or alert others to a crime. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> The whistle-blower revealed that the company was dumping chemicals into the river.
White elephant,"<b>Meaning:</b> A possession that is useless or troublesome, and expensive to maintain. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Kings of Siam supposedly gave rare white elephants to people they disliked so the cost of keeping the animal would ruin them. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> That old mansion is a white elephant; it costs more to heat than it's worth."
White lie,"<b>Meaning:</b> A small, harmless lie told to avoid hurting someone's feelings. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> 'White' symbolizes purity or lack of malice. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> I told her I liked her hat, but it was just a white lie."
Whole ball of wax,"<b>Meaning:</b> Everything; the entire thing. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Uncertain, but possibly related to an old legal practice of dividing land by drawing wax balls from a hat. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> When we moved, we decided to sell the furniture, the car—the whole ball of wax."
Wild card,"<b>Meaning:</b> A person or thing whose influence is unpredictable. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> From card games, where a specific card can be used to represent any other card. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> The new player is a wild card; we don't know how he'll perform under pressure."
Wild goose chase,<b>Meaning:</b> A search that is completely unsuccessful or a waste of time. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> From Shakespeare; originally a type of horse race where riders followed a lead horse like geese in flight. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> Finding a taxi in this storm is a wild goose chase.
Win-win situation,"<b>Meaning:</b> A result that is good for everyone involved. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> From game theory and business negotiations. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> If we trade tasks, we both finish earlier; it's a win-win situation."
Wind out of your sails,"<b>Meaning:</b> To lose your energy, confidence, or advantage suddenly. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Nautical; if a boat moves in front of yours, it 'steals' the wind, and your boat stops. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> Finding out that someone else had the same idea really took the wind out of my sails."
Wing it,"<b>Meaning:</b> To do something without preparation. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> From the theater; an actor who didn't know their lines would stay in the 'wings' of the stage and be prompted by others. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> I forgot my notes for the presentation, so I'll just have to wing it."
With a heavy heart,"<b>Meaning:</b> With a feeling of great sadness. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> A metaphor for the physical feeling of weight in the chest during grief. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> With a heavy heart, she said goodbye to her old home."
Word of mouth,<b>Meaning:</b> Information spread by people talking to each other. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> A literal description of oral communication. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> We don't advertise; all our business comes from word of mouth.
Work like a charm,"<b>Meaning:</b> To be very effective or successful. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Compares a successful action to the power of a magic spell or charm. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> I put some oil on the door hinge, and it worked like a charm."
Worth your salt,"<b>Meaning:</b> Deserving of your pay; competent and valuable. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> In ancient Rome, soldiers were sometimes paid in salt (salarium), which was very valuable. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> Any lawyer worth their salt would have noticed that error in the contract."
Wrong end of the stick,"<b>Meaning:</b> To misunderstand a situation completely. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Possibly from walking sticks; if you grab the muddy end, you've made a mistake. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> No, I’m not quitting! You’ve got the wrong end of the stick."
X marks the spot,"<b>Meaning:</b> This is the exact location of something. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> From treasure maps where an 'X' was drawn to show where the gold was buried. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> We looked everywhere for the leak, and X marks the spot—it's right under the sink."
You are what you eat,"<b>Meaning:</b> Your health is determined by the food you consume. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> First used in French by Anthelme Brillat-Savarin in 1826. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> If you only eat junk food, you'll feel terrible; you are what you eat."
You can't judge a book by its cover,"<b>Meaning:</b> You shouldn't judge someone or something by its appearance. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> First appeared in the 1940s as a reminder that the quality of a story isn't always reflected in the binding. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> He looks mean, but he’s actually very kind; you can't judge a book by its cover."
You can't teach an old dog new tricks,"<b>Meaning:</b> It is difficult to make someone change their habits or learn new things late in life. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> A literal observation about training animals, used as a metaphor for humans. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> My grandfather refuses to use a smartphone; I guess you can't teach an old dog new tricks."
"You scratch my back, and I'll scratch yours","<b>Meaning:</b> If you do a favor for me, I will do a favor for you. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> From the 17th century; a metaphor for mutual assistance in areas where one cannot reach alone. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> I’ll help you study for math if you help me with my essay; you scratch my back, and I’ll scratch yours."
Your guess is as good as mine,<b>Meaning:</b> I have no idea; I know as little as you do. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> A standard expression of shared ignorance. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> 'Why is the bus late?' 'Your guess is as good as mine.'
Zero hour,<b>Meaning:</b> The exact time when a planned (often military) event is supposed to start. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> From WWI; the moment a planned attack began. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> It's zero hour; we need to launch the website now.
Zip your lip,"<b>Meaning:</b> To stop talking or keep a secret. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> A metaphor for using a zipper to close the mouth. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> If you want to stay in this meeting, you need to zip your lip."
A day late and a dollar short,<b>Meaning:</b> Too late and not prepared enough. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Likely from the Great Depression; referring to people who couldn't pay their debts even when they tried. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> His apology was a day late and a dollar short.
Achilles' heel,"<b>Meaning:</b> A small but fatal weakness in an otherwise strong person or system. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> From Greek mythology; Achilles was dipped in the River Styx to be invulnerable, but his heel was left dry. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> Spelling was always his Achilles' heel in school."
Armed to the teeth,<b>Meaning:</b> Carrying many weapons or being fully prepared. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> From 17th-century pirates who carried so many weapons they had to hold a knife in their teeth. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> The lawyer arrived at the trial armed to the teeth with evidence.
At the end of the day,"<b>Meaning:</b> When everything is considered; the most important point. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> A metaphor for the final summary of events once the day's work is finished. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> At the end of the day, it's your decision."
Back to square one,"<b>Meaning:</b> To go back to the very beginning after a failure. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Possibly from early radio broadcasts of football games where the pitch was divided into numbered squares. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> The client rejected our design, so it's back to square one."
Bark is worse than one's bite,<b>Meaning:</b> Someone sounds much more angry or threatening than they actually are. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> An observation that a dog that barks a lot rarely bites. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> Don't worry about the coach yelling; his bark is worse than his bite.
Basket case,"<b>Meaning:</b> Someone who is extremely nervous, stressed, or unable to function. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Originally a WWI slang for soldiers who lost all their limbs and had to be carried in baskets. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> I was a total basket case before my driving test."
Beat around the bush,<b>Meaning:</b> To avoid getting to the point of a conversation. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> From hunting; beaters would hit the bushes to make birds fly out so hunters could shoot them. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> Stop beating around the bush and tell me if I'm fired.
Bite off more than one can chew,<b>Meaning:</b> To take on a task that is too big or difficult. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> A literal metaphor for putting too much food in your mouth. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> I think I bit off more than I could chew by taking five classes this semester.
Blessing in disguise,<b>Meaning:</b> Something that seems bad at first but results in something good later. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> From a 1746 hymn. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> Losing my job was a blessing in disguise because I found a much better one.
Burn your bridges,<b>Meaning:</b> To do something that makes it impossible to return to a previous situation. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> A military strategy where an army burns a bridge after crossing so they cannot retreat. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> Don't be rude when you quit; you don't want to burn your bridges.
By and large,"<b>Meaning:</b> On the whole; considering everything. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> A nautical term for how a ship sails in different wind conditions. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> By and large, the project was a success."
Call it a day,<b>Meaning:</b> To stop working for the rest of the day. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Originally 'call it a half-day' (leaving early). <br><br> <b>Example:</b> We've been working for ten hours; let's call it a day.
Chip on one's shoulder,<b>Meaning:</b> Being angry or resentful because you think you've been treated unfairly. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> From a 19th-century custom where a man would put a wood chip on his shoulder and dare someone to knock it off. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> He’s had a chip on his shoulder ever since he was passed over for the promotion.
Cold shoulder,"<b>Meaning:</b> Intentionally ignoring or being unfriendly to someone. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Serving a guest a cold piece of meat (the shoulder) instead of a warm meal as a sign they weren't welcome. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> I tried to apologize, but she gave me the cold shoulder."
Curiosity killed the cat,<b>Meaning:</b> Being too inquisitive can lead to trouble. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Originally 'Care killed the cat' (worry); 'curiosity' was substituted in the 1800s. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> Don't look in that drawer; curiosity killed the cat.
Cut to the chase,"<b>Meaning:</b> To get to the point without wasting time. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> From early silent films where the 'chase' scene was the most exciting part. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> We don't have much time, so let's cut to the chase."
Don't count your chickens before they hatch,"<b>Meaning:</b> Don't make plans based on a positive result that hasn't happened yet. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> From Aesop's fables; eggs might not all hatch into chickens. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> You haven't won the lottery yet, so don't count your chickens before they hatch."
Don't cry over spilled milk,"<b>Meaning:</b> Don't worry about a mistake that has already happened and can't be fixed. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> A literal observation that spilled milk cannot be put back in the glass. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> I know you lost the race, but there's no use crying over spilled milk."
Every dog has its day,"<b>Meaning:</b> Everyone will have a period of success or luck at some point. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> A proverb used since the 16th century. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> He hasn't won a game yet, but every dog has its day."
Fit as a fiddle,<b>Meaning:</b> In very good health. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> 'Fit' used to mean 'suitable.' A fiddle must be kept in perfect condition to sound good. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> My grandmother is 90 years old and still fit as a fiddle.
Get a taste of your own medicine,"<b>Meaning:</b> To be treated the same unpleasant way you have treated others. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> From Aesop's fables about a swindler who sold fake medicine and then got sick himself. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> He always pranks people, so it was funny to see him get a taste of his own medicine."
Give someone the benefit of the doubt,"<b>Meaning:</b> To choose to believe someone even if you aren't certain they are telling the truth. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> A legal concept where a defendant is innocent until proven guilty. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> I don't know if he's lying, but I'll give him the benefit of the doubt."
Go down in flames,<b>Meaning:</b> To fail spectacularly. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> A metaphor for a plane crash. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> The whole project went down in flames after the lead developer quit.
Hit the nail on the head,<b>Meaning:</b> To describe exactly what is causing a situation. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> From carpentry; hitting the center of the nail head is the perfect strike. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> You hit the nail on the head when you said we need more staff.
In the heat of the moment,<b>Meaning:</b> Doing or saying something without thinking because you are angry or excited. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> A metaphor for emotions 'heating up' and clouding judgment. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> I didn't mean to insult you; I just said it in the heat of the moment.
Keep something at bay,<b>Meaning:</b> To prevent something unpleasant from getting closer or worsening. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> From hunting; when a cornered animal (at bay) turns to face the dogs to keep them back. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> I take vitamins to keep the flu at bay.
Kill two birds with one stone,<b>Meaning:</b> To achieve two things with a single action. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Likely from ancient hunting using a sling. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> I'll go to the bank while I'm at the grocery store—kill two birds with one stone.
Last straw,"<b>Meaning:</b> The final problem in a series that makes you lose your patience. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> From the proverb 'the straw that broke the camel's back.' <br><br> <b>Example:</b> I was already annoyed, but when he lied to me, that was the last straw."
Let the cat out of the bag,"<b>Meaning:</b> To reveal a secret accidentally. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> From dishonest markets where a cat was sold in a bag (poke) instead of a pig. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> We were planning a surprise, but Tim let the cat out of the bag."
Make a long story short,"<b>Meaning:</b> To skip the details and get to the point. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> A literal summary. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> Long story short, we missed the flight."
Method to one's madness,"<b>Meaning:</b> Having a good reason for doing something that looks crazy. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> From Shakespeare's Hamlet. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> My desk is a mess, but there's a method to my madness."
Miss the boat,<b>Meaning:</b> To miss an opportunity. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Arriving at the dock after the ship has left. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> I waited too long to buy the tickets and missed the boat.
"No pain, no gain","<b>Meaning:</b> You must work hard or suffer to achieve something. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> A 17th-century proverb, often used in exercise. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> I’m tired of studying, but no pain, no gain."
On the fence,"<b>Meaning:</b> Undecided. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Sitting between two sides, not choosing either. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> I'm still on the fence about which car to buy."
Piece of cake,<b>Meaning:</b> Something very easy. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> From 19th-century 'cake walks' where the prize was a cake. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> That exam was a piece of cake.
Pull someone's leg,<b>Meaning:</b> To joke with someone by telling them something false. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> From the 18th century; originally meant to trip someone. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> I’m not really moving to Mars; I’m just pulling your leg.
Speak of the devil,<b>Meaning:</b> Said when the person you were just talking about appears. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> From the saying 'Speak of the devil and he shall appear.' <br><br> <b>Example:</b> Speak of the devil! Here comes Sarah now.
Steal someone's thunder,<b>Meaning:</b> To take the attention away from someone else's achievement. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> From an 18th-century playwright whose sound effect was stolen by another theater. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> She stole my thunder by announcing her news right after mine.
Take it with a grain of salt,"<b>Meaning:</b> Don't believe everything you are told. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> From an ancient Roman recipe for an antidote. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> He exaggerates, so take his stories with a grain of salt."
The best of both worlds,<b>Meaning:</b> Having the advantages of two different situations at once. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> A modern idiom for a perfect balance. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> Working part-time gives me the best of both worlds.
Through thick and thin,<b>Meaning:</b> In both good times and bad times. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> From old English 'through thicket and thin wood.' <br><br> <b>Example:</b> They have been friends through thick and thin.
Under the weather,<b>Meaning:</b> Feeling sick. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Sailors would go below deck (under the weather) when they felt seasick. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> I won't be coming to work; I'm feeling a bit under the weather.
Up in the air,<b>Meaning:</b> Unsettled or undecided. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Like an object that hasn't landed yet. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> Our vacation plans are still up in the air.
A penny for your thoughts,<b>Meaning:</b> Asking someone what they are thinking about. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> From the 16th century. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> You’ve been quiet; a penny for your thoughts?
Add insult to injury,"<b>Meaning:</b> To make a bad situation even worse. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> From an ancient Roman fable. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> I lost my job, and then to add insult to injury, my car was towed."
At the drop of a hat,<b>Meaning:</b> Doing something immediately without hesitation. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> From the 19th century when dropping a hat signaled the start of a race. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> She’s ready to travel at the drop of a hat.
Beat the clock,<b>Meaning:</b> To finish something before time runs out. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Likely from game shows or sports. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> We managed to beat the clock and submit the report at 4:59.
