Break the ice,<b>Meaning:</b> To start a conversation in a social setting. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> From 'icebreaker' ships that clear paths for others. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> I told a joke to break the ice.
Burn the midnight oil,<b>Meaning:</b> To work late into the night. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Using an oil lamp to work after dark. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> I had to burn the midnight oil to finish the project.
Cut corners,"<b>Meaning:</b> To do something poorly or illegally to save time or money. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> From driving; cutting across a corner. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> They cut corners on the construction, and now the building is unsafe."
Midas touch,<b>Meaning:</b> The ability to make a lot of money out of anything one undertakes. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Greek mythology; King Midas was granted a wish that everything he touched would turn to gold. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> Every business he starts becomes a success; he truly has the Midas touch.
Mind your P's and Q's,"<b>Meaning:</b> To be on your best behavior or to be careful with your language. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Uncertain, but possibly from bartenders tracking 'Pints and Quarts' or printers distinguishing lowercase 'p' and 'q.' <br><br> <b>Example:</b> The grandmother is very traditional, so mind your P's and Q's while visiting her."
More than one way to skin a cat,"<b>Meaning:</b> There are many different ways to achieve the same goal. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> From the 19th century; 'skinning a cat' was a common (though gruesome) metaphor for a difficult task. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> If we can't get a loan from the bank, we'll find private investors; there's more than one way to skin a cat."
Mum's the word,"<b>Meaning:</b> Keep this a secret; don't tell anyone. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> 'Mum' refers to the humming sound made with a closed mouth, representing silence. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> I'm planning a surprise party for Sarah, so mum's the word!"
Neck of the woods,"<b>Meaning:</b> A specific neighborhood or area. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Settlers in early America used 'neck' to describe a narrow strip of land or a settlement in the forest. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> If you're ever in my neck of the woods, feel free to stop by for coffee."
No love lost,"<b>Meaning:</b> Two people dislike each other intensely. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Suggests that there was never any love to begin with, so none could be 'lost' during a fight. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> There is no love lost between the two rival coaches."
No rest for the wicked,"<b>Meaning:</b> Said when you have a lot of work to do and no time to relax. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> From the Bible (Isaiah 57:21), implying that those who do wrong will never find peace. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> I have three more reports to finish tonight; no rest for the wicked!"
Not a leg to stand on,"<b>Meaning:</b> To have no evidence or justification for an argument. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> A metaphor comparing a weak argument to a body that cannot support itself. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> Without the receipt, you don't have a leg to stand on for your refund claim."
Not playing with a full deck,<b>Meaning:</b> Lacking intelligence or being mentally unstable. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> From card games; a deck with missing cards cannot be used for a proper game. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> He thinks he can talk to aliens; I suspect he's not playing with a full deck.
Off the beaten track,"<b>Meaning:</b> In a remote or unusual place, away from where most people go. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Refers to a path that has not been worn down by many travelers' feet. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> We found a tiny, amazing restaurant that was way off the beaten track."
Off the cuff,"<b>Meaning:</b> Spoken or done without preparation; extemporaneously. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Actors or speakers used to write notes on the cuffs of their shirts to refer to during a performance. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> I wasn't expecting to give a speech, so I just said a few words off the cuff."
Off the hook,"<b>Meaning:</b> No longer in trouble or responsible for something. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> From fishing; a fish that escapes the hook is free. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> Since they found the real thief, the suspect is finally off the hook."
Old hat,"<b>Meaning:</b> Something that is old-fashioned, predictable, or uninteresting because it is so familiar. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> From the 19th century; referring to an article of clothing that has been worn for a long time. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> That technology is old hat; everyone uses the new version now."
On a roll,<b>Meaning:</b> Experiencing a period of continued success or luck. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> From gambling; a player who keeps winning 'rolls' the dice repeatedly with success. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> I've closed three deals this morning; I’m really on a roll!
On the back burner,<b>Meaning:</b> Giving a task low priority for the time being. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> From cooking; the back burners of a stove are used for items that need less attention or slow simmering. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> We'll put the marketing plan on the back burner until the product is actually finished.
On the ball,"<b>Meaning:</b> Alert, knowledgeable, and quick to react. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Likely from sports like soccer or cricket, where a player must watch the ball closely. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> The new assistant is really on the ball; she caught the error before I did."
On the same page,"<b>Meaning:</b> To have the same understanding or agreement as others. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> A metaphor for a group of people reading from the same part of a book or script. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> Before we start the project, let's have a meeting to make sure we're all on the same page."
"Once bitten, twice shy","<b>Meaning:</b> A person who has been hurt once is more careful the next time. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> An 18th-century proverb about learning from painful experiences. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> I won't lend him money again; once bitten, twice shy."
Out of the frying pan into the fire,<b>Meaning:</b> Going from a bad situation to one that is even worse. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> From an ancient proverb; jumping out of a hot pan only to land in the flames. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> He quit his stressful job only to find his new boss is even meaner; out of the frying pan into the fire.
Out on a limb,"<b>Meaning:</b> In a vulnerable or risky position where you lack support. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> A metaphor for climbing a tree; the further you go onto a limb (branch), the more likely it is to break. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> I’m going out on a limb here by supporting your idea when no one else does."
Over the moon,<b>Meaning:</b> Extremely happy or delighted. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> From the nursery rhyme 'Hey Diddle Diddle' where the cow jumped over the moon. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> She was over the moon when she found out she won the scholarship.
Passing fancy,<b>Meaning:</b> A temporary interest or liking that does not last long. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> 'Fancy' is an old word for a whim or liking. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> His interest in learning the banjo was just a passing fancy.
Pay through the nose,<b>Meaning:</b> To pay an excessively high price for something. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Possibly from an ancient Viking tax where people who didn't pay had their noses slit. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> We had to pay through the nose for those front-row concert tickets.
Penny-wise and pound-foolish,<b>Meaning:</b> Careful with small amounts of money but wasteful with large amounts. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Refers to British currency (pennies and pounds). <br><br> <b>Example:</b> He drives across town to save five cents on gas but spends $100 on a dinner he doesn't need; he's penny-wise and pound-foolish.
Pick someone's brain,<b>Meaning:</b> To ask someone for information or advice because they know more about a subject. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> A metaphor for 'extracting' knowledge from another person's mind. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> Can I pick your brain about the best way to invest in stocks?
Pipe dream,<b>Meaning:</b> An unrealistic hope or plan that is unlikely to happen. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Refers to the fantasies experienced while smoking an opium pipe. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> His plan to become a millionaire by next week is just a pipe dream.
Play second fiddle,<b>Meaning:</b> To have a less important or subordinate role compared to someone else. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> From orchestras; the 'second fiddle' (violinist) supports the 'first fiddle' leader. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> I’m tired of playing second fiddle to my brother; I want to lead for once.
Pop the question,<b>Meaning:</b> To propose marriage. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> From the 18th century; the 'question' is the most important one a person can ask. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> He’s planning to pop the question during their dinner tonight.
Pot calling the kettle black,<b>Meaning:</b> Criticizing someone for a fault that you also have. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Both pots and kettles used to turn black from the soot of a fire. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> You’re calling me lazy? That’s the pot calling the kettle black!
Pull strings,<b>Meaning:</b> To use secret influence or connections to get what you want. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> A metaphor for a puppeteer controlling a marionette with strings. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> His father pulled some strings to get him an interview at the law firm.
Pushing up daisies,"<b>Meaning:</b> A humorous or lighthearted way of saying someone is dead and buried. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Suggests that the person is under the ground, providing nutrients for the flowers to grow. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> I’ll be pushing up daisies long before this project is finally finished."
Put a damper on,<b>Meaning:</b> To discourage or lessen the enjoyment of something. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> A 'damper' is a device in a piano or furnace that reduces vibration or airflow. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> The sudden rain put a damper on our picnic plans.
Put your money where your mouth is,<b>Meaning:</b> To support your words with action or a financial bet. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Challenging someone who is bragging to actually risk something on their claim. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> You say you're the best poker player? Put your money where your mouth is and join the game.
Queer the pitch,<b>Meaning:</b> To spoil someone's chances or plans. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> From 19th-century street performers; a 'pitch' was the spot where they performed. 'Queering' it meant making it unusable. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> The bad weather really queered the pitch for the outdoor concert.
Rain on someone's parade,"<b>Meaning:</b> To spoil someone's pleasure or plans. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> A literal image of a celebratory parade being ruined by bad weather. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> I don't want to rain on your parade, but the boss just rejected your proposal."
Raking it in,<b>Meaning:</b> Earning a lot of money very quickly. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> A metaphor for gathering money as if you were raking leaves into a large pile. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> Their new app is so popular they are practically raking it in.
Read the riot act,<b>Meaning:</b> To give someone a very stern warning to stop behaving badly. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> A British law from 1714 required officials to literally read the 'Riot Act' aloud to a crowd before they could be forcefully dispersed. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> The principal read the riot act to the students after they broke the school windows.
Red herring,<b>Meaning:</b> A piece of information intended to be misleading or distracting from the main issue. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Smoked herrings turn red and have a strong smell; they were supposedly used to distract hunting dogs from a scent trail. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> The politician's comments about the economy were a red herring to distract from the scandal.
Red tape,"<b>Meaning:</b> Excessive bureaucracy or adherence to rules that slows down progress. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> In the 16th century, legal and government documents were tied together with red ribbon or tape. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> It took six months to get a building permit because of all the red tape."
Ride shotgun,"<b>Meaning:</b> To sit in the front passenger seat of a vehicle. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> From the American Wild West, where the person sitting next to the stagecoach driver carried a shotgun to protect against bandits. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> I’m going to ride shotgun so I can help with the navigation."
Ring a bell,"<b>Meaning:</b> To sound familiar, but you can't quite remember the details. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Likely from psychological experiments (like Pavlov's dog) where a bell triggered a memory or reaction. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> Her name rings a bell, but I can't remember where we met."
Rise and shine,"<b>Meaning:</b> A command to wake up and get out of bed with a positive attitude. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> A military or domestic wake-up call, implying the sun is up and you should be too. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> Rise and shine! We have a long day of hiking ahead of us."
Rule the roost,"<b>Meaning:</b> To be the person in charge of a group or household. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> From a poultry farm; the dominant rooster (the one who rules the 'roost') controls all the hens. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> Even though he's the youngest, he really rules the roost in that family."
Run of the mill,<b>Meaning:</b> Ordinary or average; nothing special. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> From 19th-century manufacturing; products that came straight from the 'mill' without being specially selected or graded. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> It was just a run-of-the-mill action movie; nothing I haven't seen before.
Salt of the earth,"<b>Meaning:</b> A person who is very good, honest, and reliable. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> From the Bible (Matthew 5:13), where Jesus calls his followers the 'salt of the earth.' <br><br> <b>Example:</b> My neighbor is the salt of the earth; he's always helping people for no reason."
Saved by the bell,"<b>Meaning:</b> Rescued from a difficult situation at the very last moment. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> From boxing; a fighter who is losing is saved if the bell rings to end the round. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> The teacher was about to ask me a question I didn't know, but then the fire alarm went off; I was saved by the bell."
Scapegoat,<b>Meaning:</b> A person who is blamed for the mistakes or crimes of others. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> From a biblical ritual where a goat was symbolically burdened with the sins of the people and sent into the wilderness. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> The junior employee was made a scapegoat for the company's financial failure.
Scot free,<b>Meaning:</b> To escape without any punishment. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> A 'scot' was a medieval tax. To go 'scot-free' meant you didn't have to pay your share. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> He stole the car but got off scot-free because the police made a mistake.
See the light,"<b>Meaning:</b> To finally understand something or change your mind after being wrong. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> A metaphor for moving from the 'darkness' of ignorance into the 'light' of truth. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> After seeing the data, he finally saw the light and agreed to the new strategy."
Sell someone down the river,<b>Meaning:</b> To betray someone for your own advantage. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> From American slavery; selling a slave 'down the river' meant sending them to the harsher conditions of the deep South. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> My partner sold me down the river by taking all the credit for my work.
Set your teeth on edge,<b>Meaning:</b> To cause someone to feel intense irritation or discomfort. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> From the Bible; refers to the physical sensation in the teeth after eating something very sour. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> The sound of his voice really sets my teeth on edge.
Shed light on,<b>Meaning:</b> To provide information that makes something easier to understand. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> A literal metaphor for using a lamp to see something clearly in a dark room. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> These new documents shed light on how the accident actually happened.
Show your true colors,"<b>Meaning:</b> To reveal your real character or intentions, especially when they are bad. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> From naval warfare; ships would often fly a 'false flag' and then switch to their 'true colors' (national flag) before attacking. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> He seemed nice at first, but he showed his true colors during the argument."
Sitting duck,"<b>Meaning:</b> Someone who is in a vulnerable position and easy to attack. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> A duck sitting on the water is much easier for a hunter to hit than one that is flying. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> Without any security guards, the store was a sitting duck for thieves."
"Six of one, half a dozen of the other","<b>Meaning:</b> Two choices are basically the same; it doesn't matter which one you pick. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Half a dozen is exactly equal to six. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> We can go by train or by bus; it's six of one, half a dozen of the other."
Small fry,<b>Meaning:</b> People or things that are unimportant or insignificant. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> 'Fry' is an old word for newly hatched fish. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> The police aren't interested in the small fry; they want to catch the leader of the gang.
Sour grapes,"<b>Meaning:</b> Pretending to dislike something because you cannot have it. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> From Aesop's fables; a fox couldn't reach some grapes and decided they were probably sour anyway. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> He says he didn't want the promotion anyway, but I think it's just sour grapes."
Spick and span,"<b>Meaning:</b> Completely clean and neat. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> From 16th-century sailing; 'spick' meant a spike or nail, and 'span' meant a new wood chip. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> She spends all Saturday making sure the house is spick and span."
Square meal,"<b>Meaning:</b> A substantial, satisfying, and balanced meal. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Possibly from the British Navy, where sailors ate off square wooden plates. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> After a long day of hiking, all I wanted was a square meal."
Start from scratch,"<b>Meaning:</b> To begin something from the very beginning with no help from previous work. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> From sports like racing or cricket, where the starting line was 'scratched' into the dirt. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> The recipe failed, so I had to throw it out and start from scratch."
Stiff upper lip,"<b>Meaning:</b> Showing great courage or self-control in a difficult situation; not showing emotion. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> A trembling lip is a sign of crying; keeping it 'stiff' prevents you from showing sadness. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> Despite the bad news, he kept a stiff upper lip and continued working."
Straight from the horse's mouth,<b>Meaning:</b> Information obtained directly from the most reliable source. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> From horse racing; you can tell a horse's true age by looking at its teeth. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> I know the company is merging; I heard it straight from the horse's mouth.
Straw that broke the camel's back,"<b>Meaning:</b> The final small problem that makes a situation unbearable. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> An ancient proverb; even a single straw can break a back if it's already carrying a maximum load. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> I was already stressed, but losing my keys was the straw that broke the camel's back."
Swan song,<b>Meaning:</b> A final performance or achievement before retirement or death. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> From the ancient belief that swans are silent until they sing a beautiful song just before they die. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> This concert will be the legendary singer's swan song.
Sweeten the deal,<b>Meaning:</b> To add something extra to an offer to make it more attractive. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> A metaphor for adding sugar to something to make it taste better. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> They offered me a higher salary and a company car to sweeten the deal.
Take the bull by the horns,<b>Meaning:</b> To deal with a difficult situation directly and confidently. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> From the practice of wrestling bulls by grabbing their horns to control them. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> Stop complaining about your job and take the bull by the horns—go talk to your boss.
Take with a grain of salt,"<b>Meaning:</b> To not believe something completely; to be skeptical. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> From an ancient Roman recipe for an antidote to poison, which was supposedly more effective with a grain of salt. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> He exaggerates a lot, so take everything he says with a grain of salt."
Talk the hind legs off a donkey,"<b>Meaning:</b> To talk incessantly and for a very long time. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> A humorous exaggeration of a person's ability to keep talking until even an animal is exhausted. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> She’s very friendly, but she can talk the hind legs off a donkey."
That’s the way the cookie crumbles,"<b>Meaning:</b> That is just the way things happen, and you have to accept it. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> A 20th-century American idiom; cookies are fragile and break in unpredictable ways. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> I didn't get the job, but that’s the way the cookie crumbles."
The apple doesn't fall far from the tree,<b>Meaning:</b> Children are often very similar to their parents. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> A literal observation that fruit falls directly under the tree it grew on. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> He’s a great musician just like his father; the apple doesn't fall far from the tree.
The ball is in your court,"<b>Meaning:</b> It is your turn to take action or make a decision. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> From tennis; when the ball is on your side, you must hit it back. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> I’ve made my final offer; now the ball is in your court."
The early bird catches the worm,<b>Meaning:</b> The person who arrives or acts first will be successful. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> A proverb from the 17th century; birds that wake up early find the most food. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> We should get to the store at 8 AM for the sale; the early bird catches the worm.
The elephant in the room,"<b>Meaning:</b> A major problem or controversial issue that is present but everyone is ignoring. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> An elephant is too large to ignore, so people must be choosing not to see it. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> We need to discuss the budget cuts; it’s the elephant in the room."
The lion's share,<b>Meaning:</b> The largest or best portion of something. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> From Aesop's fables; a lion hunted with other animals but then claimed all the meat for himself. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> The eldest son received the lion's share of the money.
The proof is in the pudding,"<b>Meaning:</b> You can only judge the quality of something by trying it or seeing it in action. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Originally 'The proof of the pudding is in the eating.' <br><br> <b>Example:</b> He says the new engine is faster, but the proof is in the pudding."
The real McCoy,"<b>Meaning:</b> The genuine thing; not a copy or imitation. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Uncertain, but often attributed to Elijah McCoy, an inventor whose high-quality products were widely copied. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> This isn't a fake handbag; it’s the real McCoy."
The whole nine yards,"<b>Meaning:</b> Everything; the entire amount. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Disputed; possibly from the length of machine gun belts or the amount of fabric in a kilt. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> When he cleans the car, he does the engine, the interior, and the trunk—the whole nine yards."
Thin end of the wedge,"<b>Meaning:</b> A small, seemingly unimportant change that will lead to a much larger and more serious problem. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> A wedge is a tool that starts narrow but gets wider; once the thin end is in, it can split wood apart. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> Allowing people to work from home one day a week is the thin end of the wedge; soon no one will come to the office."
Think outside the box,<b>Meaning:</b> To think creatively or from a new perspective. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> From the 'nine dots puzzle' where you must draw lines outside the square 'box' of dots to solve it. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> We need to think outside the box to solve this marketing problem.
Third time's a charm,"<b>Meaning:</b> The third time you try something, you will be successful. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> An old superstition that the number three is lucky. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> I failed my driving test twice, but third time's a charm!"
Through the grapevine,<b>Meaning:</b> To hear news or rumors through informal conversation. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> From the 19th-century telegraph system; the tangled wires looked like grapevines. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> I heard through the grapevine that the manager is leaving.
Throw caution to the wind,<b>Meaning:</b> To behave in a risky or reckless way. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> A metaphor for throwing away your safety as if it were something light and unimportant. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> He decided to throw caution to the wind and quit his job to travel the world.
Throw in the towel,"<b>Meaning:</b> To give up or admit defeat. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> From boxing; a trainer throws a towel into the ring to stop a fight if their boxer is getting hurt. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> After trying for hours to fix the sink, I finally threw in the towel and called a plumber."
Throw someone under the bus,<b>Meaning:</b> To betray or sacrifice a friend or colleague for personal gain. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> A modern idiom; a metaphor for pushing someone into danger to save yourself. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> He threw his partner under the bus to avoid getting in trouble with the boss.
Tie the knot,<b>Meaning:</b> To get married. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> From ancient marriage ceremonies where the couple's hands were literally tied together to symbolize their union. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> They are planning to tie the knot in Hawaii next summer.
Till the cows come home,"<b>Meaning:</b> For a very long time; indefinitely. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Cows move very slowly and only return to the barn when they are ready or at the end of the day. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> You can complain until the cows come home, but I’m not changing my mind."
Tip of the iceberg,"<b>Meaning:</b> A small, visible part of a much larger problem or situation. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Most of an iceberg's mass is hidden underwater; only about 10% is visible. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> The $100 error we found is just the tip of the iceberg."
To each his own,"<b>Meaning:</b> Everyone has their own personal preferences or opinions. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> A common proverb used to express tolerance for different tastes. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> I don't like horror movies, but to each his own."
Toe the line,"<b>Meaning:</b> To follow the rules or obey orders exactly. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> From the military or sports, where people had to stand with their toes touching a literal line. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> If you want to keep your job, you'd better toe the line."
Tongue-in-cheek,"<b>Meaning:</b> Something said as a joke or sarcastically, not meant to be taken seriously. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> A physical gesture (pushing your tongue against your cheek) to hide a smile while saying something serious. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> His comment about being the smartest man in the world was strictly tongue-in-cheek."
Too many cooks spoil the broth,"<b>Meaning:</b> If too many people are involved in a task, it will not be done well. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> A 16th-century proverb; every cook adds their own flavor, eventually ruining the soup. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> We only need two people for this project; too many cooks spoil the broth."
Top notch,<b>Meaning:</b> Excellent; of the highest quality. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> From 19th-century scoring systems where 'notches' were cut into wood to track progress. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> The service at that hotel is absolutely top notch.
Touch and go,"<b>Meaning:</b> A very uncertain or risky situation. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Nautical; when a ship's hull touches the bottom but keeps moving. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> It was touch and go for a while after the surgery, but he's recovering now."
Turn a blind eye,"<b>Meaning:</b> To pretend not to notice something, usually something wrong. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Admiral Nelson supposedly held his telescope to his blind eye so he could honestly say he didn't see the signal to retreat. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> The teacher turned a blind eye to the students whispering."
Turn over a new leaf,"<b>Meaning:</b> To change your behavior for the better. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> 'Leaf' is an old word for a page in a book. Turning the page means starting a new chapter. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> After getting in trouble, he promised to turn over a new leaf and study harder."
Twist someone's arm,"<b>Meaning:</b> To persuade or pressure someone into doing something they don't want to do. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> A metaphor for physical force used to make someone obey. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> I didn't want to go to the party, but my friend twisted my arm."
Two cents' worth,"<b>Meaning:</b> Your personal opinion, especially when it is not asked for. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Refers to a very small and insignificant amount of money. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> That's just my two cents' worth, but I think the plan is a mistake."
A bitter pill to swallow,<b>Meaning:</b> An unpleasant fact or situation that must be accepted. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Refers to medicine that tastes bad but is necessary for health. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> Losing the championship by one point was a bitter pill to swallow.
A drop in the bucket,"<b>Meaning:</b> A very small amount compared to what is needed or expected. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> From the Bible (Isaiah 40:15), describing the insignificance of nations compared to the universe. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> The $10 donation is helpful, but it's just a drop in the bucket compared to the millions we need."
A house of cards,"<b>Meaning:</b> A plan or organization that is very weak and can easily fall apart. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Literally building a structure out of playing cards, which collapses with a slight breeze. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> Their business was built on lies, so it collapsed like a house of cards when the truth came out."
A left-handed compliment,"<b>Meaning:</b> A compliment that is actually an insult. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Historically, the left hand was associated with dishonesty or insincerity. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> Saying my dress 'hides my weight well' was a left-handed compliment."
A little bird told me,"<b>Meaning:</b> Used to say you heard a secret but won't reveal the source. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> From the Bible (Ecclesiastes 10:20), suggesting a bird might carry your words to the king. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> A little bird told me that you're getting promoted next month!"
A loose cannon,"<b>Meaning:</b> Someone who is unpredictable and likely to cause damage. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> From old warships where a cannon that broke free from its ropes would roll dangerously around the deck. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> He’s a bit of a loose cannon, so be careful what you say to him."
A sacred cow,"<b>Meaning:</b> An idea, custom, or institution that is held in such high regard that it cannot be criticized. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> From the Hindu belief that cows are sacred and should not be harmed. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> The company’s pension plan is a sacred cow; no one dares suggest changing it."
A stone's throw,<b>Meaning:</b> A very short distance. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> The distance a person can physically throw a stone. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> The beach is just a stone's throw from our hotel.
A storm in a teacup,<b>Meaning:</b> A lot of anger or worry about something that is not important. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Suggesting that the 'storm' is tiny because it is only happening inside a cup. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> The argument over who should wash the dishes was just a storm in a teacup.
Ace up one's sleeve,<b>Meaning:</b> A secret advantage or resource kept in reserve. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> From dishonest card players who would hide an Ace in their sleeve to use later. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> I have an ace up my sleeve for the negotiation tomorrow.
Acid test,<b>Meaning:</b> A definitive test of the value or quality of something. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Gold prospectors used nitric acid to distinguish real gold from other metals. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> The upcoming election will be the acid test for the new policy.
Against all odds,"<b>Meaning:</b> Succeeding despite very low chances of success. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> From gambling, where 'odds' represent the probability of an outcome. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> Against all odds, the small team won the national tournament."
All brawn and no brains,"<b>Meaning:</b> Physically strong but not very intelligent. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> 'Brawn' refers to muscle. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> He’s a great weightlifter, but when it comes to math, he's all brawn and no brains."
All Greek to me,"<b>Meaning:</b> Something that is completely unintelligible or impossible to understand. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> From Shakespeare’s Julius Caesar; if you didn't speak Greek, it sounded like nonsense. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> This computer manual is all Greek to me."
Apple of my eye,<b>Meaning:</b> Someone who is cherished above all others. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Ancient people believed the pupil of the eye was a solid object shaped like an apple. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> His youngest daughter is the apple of his eye.
As the crow flies,"<b>Meaning:</b> The shortest, direct distance between two points. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Crows are perceived to fly in a straight line to their destination. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> It’s 10 miles by road, but only 5 miles as the crow flies."
At a snail's pace,<b>Meaning:</b> Moving very slowly. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Snails are among the slowest creatures on Earth. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> Traffic was moving at a snail's pace due to the construction.
At loggerheads,<b>Meaning:</b> In strong disagreement or dispute. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> A 'loggerhead' was an iron tool with a bulbous end used for heating tar; sailors used them as weapons. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> The two departments are at loggerheads over the new budget.
Back-seat driver,<b>Meaning:</b> Someone who gives unwanted advice on how to do something. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> A passenger in the back of a car telling the driver how to drive. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> I don't need a back-seat driver telling me how to run my own business.
Be in someone's shoes,<b>Meaning:</b> To be in the same situation as someone else. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> A metaphor for experiencing life from another person's perspective. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> I wouldn't want to be in his shoes right now; he has so much work to do.
Beat the bushes,"<b>Meaning:</b> To search thoroughly. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> From hunting; beaters would hit bushes to make birds fly out into the open. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> I've been beating the bushes looking for a new job, but nothing has turned up yet."
Bells and whistles,"<b>Meaning:</b> Extra features added to something to make it more attractive. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> From early steam locomotives that used bells and whistles to get attention. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> The new phone has all the bells and whistles, but it’s very expensive."
Better late than never,<b>Meaning:</b> It is better for something to happen late than not at all. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> A proverb used since at least the 1200s. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> You finally arrived! Better late than never.
Big fish in a small pond,"<b>Meaning:</b> An important person in a small or unimportant organization. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> A literal comparison; a large fish looks much bigger if the pond is small. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> He was a big fish in a small pond at his old school, but now he's struggling at the university."
Birds of a feather flock together,<b>Meaning:</b> People with similar interests or characters tend to associate with each other. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> An observation from nature; birds of the same species travel in groups. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> All the musicians are hanging out together; birds of a feather flock together.
Bite the dust,"<b>Meaning:</b> To fall dead, or for something to fail or break. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> From the 18th century, referring to a soldier falling face-down into the dirt. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> My old laptop finally bit the dust this morning."
Blind as a bat,"<b>Meaning:</b> Having very poor eyesight. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Bats use echolocation rather than sight, giving the impression that they are blind. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> Without my glasses, I'm blind as a bat."
Blood is thicker than water,<b>Meaning:</b> Family relationships are stronger than any others. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Likely from the 12th century; suggesting family 'blood' is a stronger bond than any other 'water.' <br><br> <b>Example:</b> I had to help my brother even though I was busy; blood is thicker than water.
Blow your own trumpet,"<b>Meaning:</b> To boast about your own achievements. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> From the days when heralds would blow trumpets to announce the arrival of someone important. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> I don't want to blow my own trumpet, but I did get the highest score in the class."
Blue-blooded,"<b>Meaning:</b> Of noble or aristocratic birth. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> From the Spanish 'sangre azul'; aristocrats had pale skin, making their veins appear very blue. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> She comes from a blue-blooded family that has lived in this mansion for centuries."
Break a leg,"<b>Meaning:</b> A superstitious way to say 'good luck' to a performer. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> In theater, it was believed that wishing for good luck would cause bad luck. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> You'll be great in the play! Break a leg!"
Bring to light,<b>Meaning:</b> To reveal or uncover something. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Moving an object from a dark place into the light so it can be seen. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> The investigation brought to light some shocking financial errors.
Bury your head in the sand,<b>Meaning:</b> To ignore an obvious problem in the hope that it will go away. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> From the myth that ostriches hide their heads in the sand when they are scared. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> You can't just bury your head in the sand; you have to pay your bills.
Buy a pig in a poke,"<b>Meaning:</b> To buy something without looking at it or knowing its true value. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> A 'poke' was a bag; if you didn't look inside, the seller might have put a cat in the bag instead of a pig. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> Buying a used car online without seeing it is like buying a pig in a poke."
By the skin of one's teeth,"<b>Meaning:</b> To barely succeed in doing something. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> From the Book of Job in the Bible; teeth don't have skin, so the margin is non-existent. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> I passed the exam by the skin of my teeth."
Call a spade a spade,"<b>Meaning:</b> To speak plainly and directly about something, even if it is unpleasant. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Ancient Greek; 'spade' refers to a simple digging tool, implying simple language. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> Let's call a spade a spade: the company is going bankrupt."
Can't hold a candle to,"<b>Meaning:</b> To be significantly less good than someone or something else. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> An apprentice would hold a candle so a master could see their work; if they weren't even good enough for that, they were useless. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> This new movie can't hold a candle to the original."
Carry coals to Newcastle,<b>Meaning:</b> To do something that is completely unnecessary because there is already plenty of it. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Newcastle was a major coal-mining city in England; bringing coal there was pointless. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> Exporting oil to Saudi Arabia is like carrying coals to Newcastle.
Cash in one's chips,"<b>Meaning:</b> To die, or to sell your investments. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> From gambling; exchanging your betting chips for cash at the end of the game. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> He decided to cash in his chips and retire to Florida."
Cast in the same mold,<b>Meaning:</b> To be very similar in character or appearance. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> From metalworking; objects poured into the same mold come out identical. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> Both brothers are cast in the same mold: hardworking and honest.
Cat got your tongue?,"<b>Meaning:</b> Why are you not saying anything? <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Uncertain, but possibly related to the 'cat-o'-nine-tails' whip used for punishment. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> You’ve been silent since I asked you the question. Cat got your tongue?"
Caught between a rock and a hard place,"<b>Meaning:</b> Facing two equally difficult or unpleasant choices. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> A 20th-century American idiom for being in a dilemma. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> I'm caught between a rock and a hard place: if I quit, I have no money, but if I stay, I'm miserable."
Chew the cud,"<b>Meaning:</b> To think deeply about something. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Cows 'chew the cud' (re-chew their food) for a long time, looking very thoughtful. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> I need some time to chew the cud before I make a decision."
Chicken out,"<b>Meaning:</b> To decide not to do something because you are afraid. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Chickens have long been symbols of cowardice. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> I was going to go bungee jumping, but I chickened out at the last second."
Clean as a whistle,"<b>Meaning:</b> Completely clean, or completely legal. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Possibly from the clear, 'clean' sound a whistle makes. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> The police searched his house but found nothing; his record is clean as a whistle."
Close shave,"<b>Meaning:</b> A narrow escape from danger. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> If a barber shaves you very closely, it is barely safe for the skin. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> That car almost hit me! That was a close shave."
Cock and bull story,<b>Meaning:</b> An unlikely and unbelievable story used as an excuse. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Likely from two rival inns in England where travelers told tall tales. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> He gave me some cock and bull story about why he didn't have his homework.
Come hell or high water,"<b>Meaning:</b> No matter what obstacles or difficulties occur. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Likely from the 19th-century American West; referring to destructive fires or floods. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> I’ll be there for your wedding, come hell or high water."
Cook the books,<b>Meaning:</b> To dishonestly change financial records to steal money or avoid taxes. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> 'Cook' is slang for manipulating or falsifying. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> The accountant was arrested for cooking the books.
Cool as a cucumber,"<b>Meaning:</b> Very calm and composed, especially in a stressful situation. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> The inside of a cucumber stays significantly cooler than the air temperature. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> Even though the building was on fire, he remained cool as a cucumber."
Crocodile tears,<b>Meaning:</b> False or insincere tears. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> From the ancient myth that crocodiles weep while they eat their prey. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> She shed crocodile tears when she heard her rival lost the job.
Cross that bridge when you come to it,"<b>Meaning:</b> Deal with a problem only when it actually happens, not before. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> A reminder not to worry about future difficulties. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> We don't know if it will rain tomorrow, so let's just cross that bridge when we come to it."
Cry wolf,"<b>Meaning:</b> To give a false alarm so many times that people stop believing you. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> From Aesop's fable about a boy who lied about a wolf attacking his sheep. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> If you keep complaining about small pains, no one will help you when you really get hurt; don't cry wolf."
Dark horse,<b>Meaning:</b> A person who unexpectedly wins or succeeds. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> From horse racing; a horse that isn't known to the gamblers. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> No one expected him to win the election; he was a real dark horse.
Dead as a doornail,"<b>Meaning:</b> Completely dead or unusable. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Nails in old doors were hammered through and then bent over so they couldn't be reused; they were 'dead.' <br><br> <b>Example:</b> I tried to start the car, but the battery is dead as a doornail."
Devil's advocate,"<b>Meaning:</b> Someone who takes the opposite side in an argument to test the strength of the idea. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> From the Catholic Church; a person appointed to argue against a candidate for sainthood. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> I agree with you, but let me play devil's advocate for a moment."
Diamond in the rough,"<b>Meaning:</b> Someone who has great potential but lacks social polish or education. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> A raw diamond that has not yet been cut or polished. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> The new intern is a bit messy, but she's a diamond in the rough."
Dog-eat-dog,<b>Meaning:</b> A situation of fierce competition where people will do anything to succeed. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> From the belief that dogs are so vicious they would eat each other. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> It's a dog-eat-dog world in the music industry.
Don't put the cart before the horse,<b>Meaning:</b> Don't do things in the wrong order. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> A cart cannot move if the horse is behind it. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> You’re already planning the honeymoon but you haven't even proposed; don't put the cart before the horse.
Down and out,"<b>Meaning:</b> Having no money, job, or hope. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> From boxing; a fighter who is 'down' on the canvas and 'out' of the fight. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> After he lost his business, he was completely down and out."
Draw the line,"<b>Meaning:</b> To set a limit on what you will allow. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Likely from marking boundaries in soil or on a field. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> I'll help you with the chores, but I draw the line at cleaning your room."
Drive a hard bargain,"<b>Meaning:</b> To be very demanding during a negotiation to get the best deal. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> 'Drive' implies force or effort. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> She drives a hard bargain, but we finally agreed on a price."
Drop the ball,<b>Meaning:</b> To make a mistake or fail to do your duty. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> From sports like football or baseball where dropping the ball is a failure. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> I really dropped the ball by forgetting to call the client back.
Dutch courage,<b>Meaning:</b> False courage gained from drinking alcohol. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> English sailors in the 17th century believed Dutch sailors only fought well when they were drunk. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> He needed a bit of Dutch courage before he could go on stage and give his speech.
"Easy come, easy go","<b>Meaning:</b> Something that is easily gained is also easily lost. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> A 19th-century proverb about the nature of wealth or luck. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> I won $50 and spent it all an hour later. Easy come, easy go."
Eat humble pie,"<b>Meaning:</b> To admit you were wrong and apologize. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> 'Umble pie' was made from the offal (organs) of a deer and served to the lower classes. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> After bragging about his win, he had to eat humble pie when the results were corrected."
Eleventh hour,<b>Meaning:</b> The latest possible moment. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> From the Bible; the last hour of a 12-hour workday. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> The two countries reached a peace agreement at the eleventh hour.
Every cloud has a silver lining,"<b>Meaning:</b> There is something good in every bad situation. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> From a John Milton poem; even a dark cloud has a bright edge where the sun hits it. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> I lost my job, but it gave me time to start my own business; every cloud has a silver lining."
Face the music,"<b>Meaning:</b> To accept the unpleasant consequences of your actions. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Likely from the military practice of drumming a soldier out of the regiment. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> After breaking the window, I had to go home and face the music."
Fair-weather friend,<b>Meaning:</b> A friend who is only there when things are going well. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> A nautical metaphor; someone who sails with you only when the weather is good. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> He disappeared as soon as I lost my money; he was just a fair-weather friend.
Fall on one's sword,<b>Meaning:</b> To take the blame for something or resign to protect others. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Ancient Roman generals would literally kill themselves with their swords to avoid the shame of defeat. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> The manager decided to fall on his sword and take responsibility for the failed project.
Feather in your cap,<b>Meaning:</b> An achievement you can be proud of. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> From the custom of warriors adding a feather to their headgear for every enemy they defeated. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> Getting your article published in a national magazine is a real feather in your cap.
Feel the pinch,"<b>Meaning:</b> To experience hardship because of a lack of money. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> A metaphor for the 'tightness' of a small budget. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> With the new taxes, many families are starting to feel the pinch."
Fine-tooth comb,<b>Meaning:</b> To examine something in great detail. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Using a comb with teeth very close together to find small things like lice. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> The lawyers went through the contract with a fine-tooth comb.
Fish out of water,"<b>Meaning:</b> Someone who is in an unfamiliar and uncomfortable situation. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> A fish cannot survive or behave normally on land. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> At the fancy gala, I felt like a fish out of water."
Fly on the wall,<b>Meaning:</b> An unnoticed observer of a private conversation or event. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Suggests being as small and insignificant as a fly so no one notices you are there. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> I'd love to be a fly on the wall when they discuss the new promotions.
Follow suit,"<b>Meaning:</b> To do the same thing as someone else. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> From card games, where you must play a card of the same suit as the first player. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> When the first company lowered its prices, all the others followed suit."
From the horse's mouth,"<b>Meaning:</b> Directly from the original or most reliable source. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> From horse racing; a horse’s teeth can tell you its true age, regardless of what the seller says. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> I know the company is closing; I heard it straight from the horse's mouth."
Get off on the wrong foot,<b>Meaning:</b> To start a relationship or a task badly. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> From the ancient superstition that starting to walk with the left foot brought bad luck. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> I think we got off on the wrong foot; let's start our meeting again.
Get your ducks in a row,<b>Meaning:</b> To get well-organized and prepared. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Likely from bowling; the pins used to be called 'ducks' and had to be reset in a row. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> We need to get our ducks in a row before we pitch to the investors.
Gift of the gab,<b>Meaning:</b> The ability to speak eloquently and persuasively. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> 'Gab' is an old word for chatter; possibly linked to the Blarney Stone in Ireland. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> He has the gift of the gab; he could sell a heater to someone in the desert.
Give someone the slip,<b>Meaning:</b> To escape from someone who is following you. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> From the 16th century; 'slip' means to move quietly or smoothly. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> The thief managed to give the police the slip in the crowded market.
Go the whole hog,"<b>Meaning:</b> To do something completely and thoroughly. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Possibly from an old word for a coin (a hog), meaning to spend the whole thing. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> If you're going to renovate the kitchen, you might as well go the whole hog and do the bathroom too."
Green-eyed monster,<b>Meaning:</b> Jealousy. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Coined by Shakespeare in Othello; green was associated with illness and bile. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> He saw the green-eyed monster when his friend got the promotion he wanted.
Hard nut to crack,<b>Meaning:</b> A problem that is difficult to solve or a person who is difficult to understand. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> A literal comparison to a nut with a very tough shell. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> This physics equation is a real hard nut to crack.
Harp on,"<b>Meaning:</b> To talk about the same subject repeatedly and annoyingly. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> From playing a harp; repeatedly plucking the same string. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> I know I made a mistake, but you don't have to keep harping on it."
Have a card up your sleeve,<b>Meaning:</b> To have a secret plan or advantage. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Cheating at cards by hiding one in a sleeve. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> Don't worry about the competition; I have a card up my sleeve.
Have the last laugh,"<b>Meaning:</b> To succeed in the end after people previously doubted you. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> A proverb dating back to the 1600s. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> Everyone mocked his invention, but he had the last laugh when it became a global success."
In a nutshell,"<b>Meaning:</b> Summarized in a very few words. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> From the idea of writing a story so small it could fit inside a nutshell. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> In a nutshell, we need more money and more time."
Keep your chin up,"<b>Meaning:</b> To stay positive in a difficult situation. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> A physical metaphor for pride and hope; looking down is a sign of sadness. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> I know you're disappointed, but keep your chin up; things will get better."
Know the ropes,<b>Meaning:</b> To be experienced and know how a system works. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> From sailing; sailors had to know which ropes controlled which sails. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> Ask the supervisor; she’s been here 20 years and knows the ropes.
Level playing field,"<b>Meaning:</b> A situation where everyone has the same opportunity to succeed. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> In sports, if a field is slanted, one team has an unfair advantage. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> Tax cuts for small businesses would create a more level playing field."
Lion's share,<b>Meaning:</b> The biggest or best part of something. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> From Aesop's fables where a lion claimed all the meat for himself. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> The eldest son received the lion's share of the inheritance.
Make no bones about it,"<b>Meaning:</b> To be very direct and honest about something. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> From 'finding bones in soup'; if there are no bones, you can eat it without hesitation or difficulty. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> He made no bones about the fact that he hated the new boss."
Hard feelings,<b>Meaning:</b> Feelings of anger or resentment after a dispute. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Refers to emotions that have 'hardened' into a grudge rather than being forgiven. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> I hope there are no hard feelings after our argument yesterday.
Have a bone to pick,"<b>Meaning:</b> To have a complaint to discuss with someone. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> From the 16th century; refers to a dog chewing on a bone for a long time, just as a person might 'chew' on a grievance. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> I have a bone to pick with you regarding the mess you left in the kitchen."
Have a field day,"<b>Meaning:</b> To have a lot of fun or success doing something you enjoy, often at someone else's expense. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Originally a military term for a day of maneuvers or exercise in the field. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> The press had a field day when the celebrity was caught in a scandal."
Have a heart of gold,"<b>Meaning:</b> To be extremely kind and generous. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Comparing a person's character to gold, the most precious and 'pure' metal. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> She spends all her weekends volunteering; she truly has a heart of gold."
Have your cake and eat it too,"<b>Meaning:</b> To have two desirable things that are usually impossible to have at the same time. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> A literal observation; once you eat the cake, you no longer possess it. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> He wants a high-paying job but doesn't want to work any overtime; he wants to have his cake and eat it too."
Head in the clouds,"<b>Meaning:</b> Being unrealistic or not paying attention to what is happening around you. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> A metaphor for someone whose mind is far above the 'ground' of reality. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> He’s always got his head in the clouds, dreaming of becoming a rock star."
Heard it through the grapevine,"<b>Meaning:</b> To learn news or rumors through informal conversation rather than official sources. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> From the tangled appearance of telegraph wires in the 1800s, which resembled grape vines. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> I heard it through the grapevine that the manager is quitting."
High and dry,"<b>Meaning:</b> Left in a difficult situation without help. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> A nautical term for a ship that is stranded on the shore after the tide goes out. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> When the partner left the business, he was left high and dry."
Hit the books,"<b>Meaning:</b> To study hard. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> A metaphor for physically starting to work with textbooks. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> I have a final exam tomorrow, so I really need to hit the books tonight."
Hit the ceiling,<b>Meaning:</b> To become very angry. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Suggests that a person's temper is so explosive they physically 'rise' to the top of the room. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> My father hit the ceiling when he saw the dent in his car.
Hit the road,<b>Meaning:</b> To begin a journey or leave a place. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> A literal metaphor for tires or feet 'hitting' the pavement of a road. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> It’s getting late; it's time for us to hit the road.
Hold your horses,<b>Meaning:</b> To wait or slow down; be patient. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> From the days of horse-drawn carriages; literally pulling the reins to stop the horses. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> Hold your horses! We aren't ready to leave yet.
Ignorance is bliss,"<b>Meaning:</b> It is better not to know about something if it would make you unhappy. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> From a 1742 poem by Thomas Gray. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> I didn't know the house was haunted when I bought it, and honestly, ignorance is bliss."
In a jam,<b>Meaning:</b> To be in a difficult situation. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Refers to being physically stuck or 'jammed' so that movement is impossible. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> I’m in a bit of a jam because my car won't start and I have a meeting.
In a pickle,<b>Meaning:</b> To be in a troublesome or difficult situation. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Possibly from 'pickled' vegetables being trapped in a jar of vinegar. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> We’re in a real pickle now that we've lost the map.
In the bag,"<b>Meaning:</b> Certain to be won or achieved. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> From 19th-century hunting, where the day's catch was placed in a bag. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> With our star player back, the championship is in the bag."
In the dark,<b>Meaning:</b> Unaware of something; not having information. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> A metaphor where 'light' equals knowledge and 'darkness' equals ignorance. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> They kept me in the dark about the layoffs until the very last day.
In the limelight,"<b>Meaning:</b> At the center of public attention. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Limelight was a type of stage lighting used in theaters before electricity. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> Ever since his book became a bestseller, he’s been in the limelight."
In the red,<b>Meaning:</b> Spending more money than you are earning; in debt. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Accountants traditionally used red ink to record losses or debts. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> The company has been in the red for three consecutive quarters.
It’s not rocket science,<b>Meaning:</b> Something is not difficult to understand. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Compares the task at hand to the extreme complexity of aerospace engineering. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> Just follow the instructions; it’s not rocket science.
Jump the gun,"<b>Meaning:</b> To do something too soon, before the right time. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> From track races where a runner starts before the starting pistol is fired. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> We jumped the gun by announcing the product before it was finished."
Keep an eye on,<b>Meaning:</b> To watch or monitor something carefully. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> A metaphor for focused observation. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> Can you keep an eye on the soup while I’m out of the room?
Keep someone at arm’s length,<b>Meaning:</b> To avoid becoming too friendly or close with someone. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Maintaining a physical distance to prevent intimacy or conflict. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> I try to keep my coworkers at arm’s length to avoid office drama.
Keep your fingers crossed,<b>Meaning:</b> To hope for good luck. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> An ancient superstition that crossing fingers would trap good spirits or ward off bad luck. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> I’m keeping my fingers crossed that I get the job.
Kick the bucket,"<b>Meaning:</b> A euphemism for dying. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Disputed, but possibly from a person standing on a bucket to hang themselves and سپس kicking it away. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> He lived a long life before he finally kicked the bucket at age 98."
Knock on wood,"<b>Meaning:</b> Tapping on something made of wood to avoid bad luck. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> From ancient beliefs that spirits lived in trees and tapping them would protect you. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> I haven't been sick all year, knock on wood."
Lend a hand,<b>Meaning:</b> To help someone. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> A physical metaphor for offering one's manual labor. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> Could you lend a hand with these heavy boxes?
Let bygones be bygones,<b>Meaning:</b> To forget past disagreements and move on. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> 'Bygone' means something that has gone by or passed. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> They decided to let bygones be bygones and start their friendship over.
Like a fish out of water,"<b>Meaning:</b> Feeling uncomfortable or awkward in a new environment. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> A fish cannot survive or function properly outside of water. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> As a city girl in the countryside, I felt like a fish out of water."
Long in the tooth,"<b>Meaning:</b> Being old. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> As horses age, their gums recede, making their teeth appear longer. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> That car is getting a bit long in the tooth; it might be time for a new one."
Lose your touch,"<b>Meaning:</b> To lose an ability or talent you once had. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> A metaphor for no longer having the 'feel' or sensitivity for a skill. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> He used to be a great cook, but he’s losing his touch."
Make a mountain out of a molehill,<b>Meaning:</b> To exaggerate a small problem and make it seem like a major one. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Compares a tiny mound of dirt (molehill) to a massive mountain. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> It was just a small mistake; don't make a mountain out of a molehill.
Make ends meet,"<b>Meaning:</b> To earn enough money to pay for your basic expenses. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> From the 17th century; possibly referring to the ends of a year's income and expenses meeting. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> With the rent increase, it’s hard to make ends meet."
Method to my madness,"<b>Meaning:</b> Having a specific plan or reason for doing something that seems crazy or disorganized. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> From Shakespeare's Hamlet. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> My filing system looks messy, but there is a method to my madness."
Money doesn't grow on trees,<b>Meaning:</b> Money is limited and must be worked for; it is not easily acquired. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> A literal observation that money (unlike fruit) is not a natural resource you can just pick. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> Turn off the lights; money doesn't grow on trees.
Never bite the hand that feeds you,"<b>Meaning:</b> Do not hurt or be ungrateful to someone who helps or supports you. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> An observation of dog behavior; a loyal dog would never bite its owner. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> Even if you disagree with your boss, don't bite the hand that feeds you."
Nutty as a fruitcake,<b>Meaning:</b> Very crazy or eccentric. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Fruitcakes are literally full of nuts; 'nutty' is slang for crazy. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> That old man’s stories are wild; he’s nutty as a fruitcake.
On cloud nine,"<b>Meaning:</b> Extremely happy and excited. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> From early cloud classifications where 'Cloud 9' was the highest, loftiest type. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> She’s been on cloud nine since she got engaged."
On thin ice,"<b>Meaning:</b> In a risky or dangerous situation where a mistake could lead to disaster. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> A literal metaphor for walking on ice that might break at any moment. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> After being late for the third time, he is on thin ice with the manager."
Painting the town red,"<b>Meaning:</b> Going out and celebrating wildly. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Possibly from an 1837 incident where a group of men literally painted buildings red during a night of rowdy drinking. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> It’s her 21st birthday, so we’re going to paint the town red."
Pass the buck,<b>Meaning:</b> To shift responsibility or blame to someone else. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> From poker; a knife with a buckhorn handle (the 'buck') was passed to the person whose turn it was to deal. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> Don't try to pass the buck; it was your job to lock the door.
Pull someone’s leg,<b>Meaning:</b> To tease or joke with someone by telling them something untrue. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Historically meant to trip someone; now refers to 'tripping' them with a joke. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> I didn't really see a UFO; I was just pulling your leg.
Right as rain,"<b>Meaning:</b> Feeling completely well or healthy. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> 'Right' once meant 'straight' or 'fitting'; rain is seen as a natural, correct occurrence. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> I was sick yesterday, but I'm as right as rain today."
Run out of steam,"<b>Meaning:</b> To lose energy or interest in something. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> From steam engines that stop moving when the steam pressure drops. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> The project started well, but we ran out of steam by the end of the month."
Sell like hotcakes,"<b>Meaning:</b> To be bought quickly and in large numbers. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> 'Hotcakes' (pancakes) are best when fresh and hot, so they sell very fast at fairs or markets. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> The new video game is selling like hotcakes."
Set the record straight,<b>Meaning:</b> To correct a mistake or provide the true facts about a situation. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> From the idea of correcting a formal written record. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> I want to set the record straight: I did not quit; I was laid off.
Shot in the dark,"<b>Meaning:</b> A random guess or attempt without much hope of success. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> A literal metaphor for firing a weapon when you cannot see the target. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> I didn't know the answer, so my guess was just a shot in the dark."
Skin of your teeth,<b>Meaning:</b> To barely manage to do something. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> From the Book of Job in the Bible. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> I passed the class by the skin of my teeth.
Time flies,<b>Meaning:</b> Time passes very quickly. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> From the Latin 'tempus fugit.' <br><br> <b>Example:</b> I can't believe your son is already ten; time flies!
To cost an arm and a leg,<b>Meaning:</b> To be very expensive. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Possibly from the price of portraits in the 18th century. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> This new laptop cost me an arm and a leg.
To cut corners,"<b>Meaning:</b> To do something poorly to save time or money. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> From driving; cutting across a corner rather than following the road. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> They cut corners on the construction, and now the roof leaks."
To feel under the weather,<b>Meaning:</b> To feel sick. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Sailors would go below deck (under the weather) when they felt seasick. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> I think I’ll stay home today; I’m feeling a bit under the weather.
To hit the nail on the head,<b>Meaning:</b> To describe exactly what is causing a situation. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> From carpentry; hitting a nail perfectly on its head. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> You hit the nail on the head when you said the problem is a lack of communication.
To kill time,<b>Meaning:</b> To do something to keep busy while waiting. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> A metaphor for 'destroying' the minutes so they pass faster. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> I played games on my phone to kill time at the airport.
To let the cat out of the bag,<b>Meaning:</b> To reveal a secret by accident. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> From 18th-century street markets. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> I accidentally let the cat out of the bag about the surprise.
To make a long story short,"<b>Meaning:</b> To get to the point. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> A literal summary. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> Long story short, we got lost and missed the party."
To steal someone's thunder,<b>Meaning:</b> To take credit for someone else's work or take attention away from them. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> From an 18th-century playwright whose thunder-making machine was copied by another theater. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> She stole my thunder by announcing her pregnancy at my wedding.
Under the sun,<b>Meaning:</b> Everything on Earth. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> From the Bible (Ecclesiastes). <br><br> <b>Example:</b> We talked about everything under the sun.
A toss-up,<b>Meaning:</b> A situation where two outcomes are equally likely. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> From the practice of tossing a coin to decide a result. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> Both teams are playing so well that the final result is a toss-up.
Against the grain,<b>Meaning:</b> Doing something that is contrary to one's natural tendencies or social norms. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> From woodworking; it is harder to cut wood across the natural fiber (the grain) than with it. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> It goes against the grain for me to lie to my parents.
All in a day's work,<b>Meaning:</b> Part of what is expected and normal in one's job. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Suggests that even difficult or unusual tasks are just standard routine. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> Helping people escape the fire is all in a day's work for a firefighter.
As a rule of thumb,"<b>Meaning:</b> A broadly accurate guide or principle based on experience rather than theory. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Historically, people used the width of their thumb as a rough measurement for length. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> As a rule of thumb, you should save 20% of your income."
At the eleventh hour,"<b>Meaning:</b> At the latest possible moment. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> From the Bible (Parable of the Workers in the Vineyard), where the last group was hired at the 11th hour of a 12-hour workday. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> The deal was signed at the eleventh hour, just before the deadline."
At the end of your tether,"<b>Meaning:</b> Having no patience or energy left to deal with a difficult situation. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Refers to an animal tied to a rope (tether) that has reached its physical limit of movement. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> Between the crying baby and the broken sink, I’m at the end of my tether."
Back to the salt mines,"<b>Meaning:</b> Returning to work, usually with a sense of reluctance. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Historically, being sent to work in salt mines was a punishment for prisoners. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> My vacation is over; tomorrow it's back to the salt mines."
Behind the eight ball,"<b>Meaning:</b> In a difficult or disadvantageous position. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> From the game of pool; if the cue ball is behind the black 8-ball, the shot is nearly impossible. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> After starting the project late, we are really behind the eight ball."
Below the belt,<b>Meaning:</b> A remark or action that is unfair or cruel. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> From boxing; hitting an opponent below the waistline is illegal and unsportsmanlike. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> Bringing up his past failures during the argument was a bit below the belt.
Beside oneself,"<b>Meaning:</b> Overcome with a very strong emotion, like grief or anger. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> From the ancient idea that a person's soul could literally leave their body during extreme emotion. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> She was beside herself with joy when she heard the news."
Best thing since sliced bread,<b>Meaning:</b> A person or thing that is thought to be excellent or innovative. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Sliced bread was first sold in 1928 and was considered a major convenience. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> This new smartphone app is the best thing since sliced bread.
Break the bank,"<b>Meaning:</b> To cost more money than one can afford. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> From gambling; when a player wins more money than the casino (the bank) has on the table. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> We can go out to dinner, as long as it doesn't break the bank."
Can't make an omelet without breaking eggs,"<b>Meaning:</b> It is impossible to achieve something important without causing some problems or sacrifices. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Attributed to several 18th-century French figures. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> The construction is noisy, but you can't make an omelet without breaking eggs."
Cash in your chips,"<b>Meaning:</b> To sell something for profit, or (euphemistically) to die. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> From gambling; exchanging betting chips for actual money at the end of a game. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> He decided to cash in his chips and sell his shares while the market was high."
Checkered past,<b>Meaning:</b> A background filled with both successes and failures or scandals. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> From a chessboard; the alternating black and white squares represent good and bad periods. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> The politician struggled to get elected because of his checkered past.
Climb the walls,"<b>Meaning:</b> To be extremely frustrated, anxious, or bored. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Suggests being so trapped by emotion that one wants to physically escape by climbing. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> Being stuck inside during the rainstorm made the kids start climbing the walls."
Cold feet,"<b>Meaning:</b> To become nervous and lose courage to do something planned. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Possibly from an 1800s novel, or simply the physical feeling of blood leaving the extremities due to fear. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> He was supposed to go skydiving, but he got cold feet at the last minute."
Cool your heels,"<b>Meaning:</b> To be kept waiting. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> From the 16th century, referring to horses whose 'heels' (hooves) became hot from running and needed to cool down. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> They let him cool his heels in the waiting room for an hour."
Cry over spilled milk,"<b>Meaning:</b> Worrying about a past mistake that cannot be undone. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> A literal metaphor; once milk is spilled, it cannot be gathered back into the glass. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> I know you lost the money, but there’s no use crying over spilled milk."
Cut someone some slack,"<b>Meaning:</b> To be less strict or critical with someone. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Nautical; giving 'slack' to a rope means it isn't tight and allows for movement. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> He’s new at the job, so cut him some slack if he makes a mistake."
Don't hold your breath,"<b>Meaning:</b> Don't expect something to happen anytime soon. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> A warning that if you held your breath until the event happened, you would suffocate. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> He said he’d pay me back today, but I’m not holding my breath."
Down for the count,"<b>Meaning:</b> Tired, defeated, or unconscious. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> From boxing; if a fighter is knocked down, the referee counts to ten. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> After the long hike, I was down for the count by 8 PM."
Down in the dumps,<b>Meaning:</b> Feeling unhappy or depressed. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> 'Dumps' comes from an old word for a melancholy state or dazed state. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> He’s been a bit down in the dumps since his girlfriend moved away.
Dressed to the nines,"<b>Meaning:</b> Dressed in a very fashionable or formal way. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Uncertain, but possibly from the use of nine yards of fabric for an expensive suit. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> Everyone was dressed to the nines for the charity gala."
Eagle eye,<b>Meaning:</b> Excellent vision or the ability to notice small details. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Eagles are famous for their incredibly sharp eyesight used for hunting. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> The editor has an eagle eye for spotting spelling mistakes.
Easy as pie,"<b>Meaning:</b> Something that is very simple to do. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Refers to how easy it is to eat a pie, rather than how easy it is to bake one. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> After studying all night, the test was easy as pie."
Face-to-face,"<b>Meaning:</b> In person; directly in front of each other. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> A literal description of two people looking at each other. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> We need to discuss this face-to-face, not over email."
Fair and square,<b>Meaning:</b> Honestly and according to the rules. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> 'Square' in the 16th century meant honest or equitable. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> We won the game fair and square.
Fall on deaf ears,<b>Meaning:</b> To be ignored or not noticed. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> A metaphor for speaking to someone who refuses to listen. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> My suggestions for improving the office fell on deaf ears.
