Feather your own nest,"<b>Meaning:</b> To make yourself richer, especially in a dishonest way. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> From birds using feathers to make their nests comfortable for themselves. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> He was accused of feathering his own nest with company funds."
Feel like a million bucks,"<b>Meaning:</b> To feel very healthy and happy. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Suggests that the person feels as valuable as a large sum of money. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> After a good night's sleep, I feel like a million bucks."
Few and far between,<b>Meaning:</b> Rare or infrequent. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> From a 1799 poem; refers to something spaced widely apart. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> Good opportunities like this are few and far between.
Fight tooth and nail,<b>Meaning:</b> To fight very fiercely or with all one's strength. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Using everything available—including biting and scratching—to win. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> She fought tooth and nail to keep her business from closing.
Fish for compliments,"<b>Meaning:</b> To try to get people to say nice things about you. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> A metaphor for 'casting a line' to catch a compliment. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> When she says she looks 'terrible,' she's just fishing for compliments."
Foot in the door,<b>Meaning:</b> A small but successful start in an activity that may lead to something bigger. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> From door-to-door salesmen who would literally put a foot in the door so it couldn't be closed. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> Taking an internship is a good way to get your foot in the door at that company.
Full of beans,<b>Meaning:</b> Having a lot of energy and enthusiasm. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Refers to horses that were fed beans (high energy food) and became lively. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> The kids were full of beans after the birthday party.
Get a grip,<b>Meaning:</b> To take control of one's emotions. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> A metaphor for physically holding onto one's self-control so it doesn't 'slip.' <br><br> <b>Example:</b> You need to get a grip and stop panicking.
Get someone's goat,"<b>Meaning:</b> To annoy or irritate someone. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> From horse racing; goats were placed in stalls with horses to calm them down. If someone stole the goat, the horse would become upset. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> It really gets my goat when people talk loudly on their phones in the library."
Get the hang of something,"<b>Meaning:</b> To learn how to do something properly. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Likely from using tools like axes or scythes, which have a specific 'hang' or balance. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> It took me a while, but I’m finally getting the hang of this software."
Get the show on the road,<b>Meaning:</b> To start an activity or journey. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> From traveling circuses and theater troupes moving to the next town. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> We have everything packed; let's get the show on the road.
Get your feet wet,<b>Meaning:</b> To start doing something for the first time. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> A metaphor for stepping into water to see how cold it is before swimming. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> He took a small role in the play just to get his feet wet.
Give someone the cold shoulder,"<b>Meaning:</b> To intentionally ignore or be unfriendly toward someone. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> From the custom of serving an unwanted guest a cold piece of meat instead of a warm meal. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> I tried to say hello, but she gave me the cold shoulder."
Give up the ghost,<b>Meaning:</b> To stop working or to die. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> From the Bible; the 'ghost' refers to the soul or spirit leaving the body. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> My old car finally gave up the ghost on the highway.
Go bananas,"<b>Meaning:</b> To become very angry, crazy, or excited. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Likely from monkeys becoming wild when they see bananas. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> The crowd went bananas when the band finally came on stage."
Go bust,"<b>Meaning:</b> To lose all one's money and go bankrupt. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> From 'burst,' meaning to break apart under pressure. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> Many small businesses went bust during the economic crisis."
Go cold turkey,"<b>Meaning:</b> To stop a habit suddenly and completely. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Possibly from the cold, clammy skin and 'goosebumps' associated with drug withdrawal. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> He decided to quit smoking cold turkey."
Go for broke,<b>Meaning:</b> To risk everything in an all-out effort. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> From Hawaiian gambling slang meaning to bet everything. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> We decided to go for broke and invest all our savings into the startup.
Go with the flow,<b>Meaning:</b> To accept a situation as it is and move with it rather than resisting. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> A metaphor for moving with the current of a river. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> I don't have a plan for the weekend; I’m just going to go with the flow.
Grass is always greener on the other side,"<b>Meaning:</b> Other people's situations always seem better than one's own. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> From cattle looking at a neighboring field and thinking the grass there is better. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> He quit his job for a 'better' one, but soon realized the grass isn't always greener."
Graveyard shift,"<b>Meaning:</b> A work shift that runs through the middle of the night. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Uncertain, but associated with the quiet and darkness of a graveyard at night. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> My brother works the graveyard shift at the hospital."
Grease someone's palm,<b>Meaning:</b> To bribe someone. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> From the 16th century; 'greasing' makes something move more smoothly. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> He had to grease the guard's palm to get into the building.
Great minds think alike,<b>Meaning:</b> Used when two people have the same thought at the same time. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> A 17th-century proverb suggesting intelligence leads to similar conclusions. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> 'I was just about to suggest pizza.' 'Great minds think alike!'
Green thumb,<b>Meaning:</b> A natural talent for growing plants. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Algae from handling pots or plants can literally turn a gardener's thumb green. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> My mother has a real green thumb; her garden is beautiful.
Hands down,<b>Meaning:</b> Easily or without question. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> From horse racing; if a jockey is so far ahead they can win with their hands down (not pulling the reins). <br><br> <b>Example:</b> She is hands down the best singer in the competition.
Happy camper,"<b>Meaning:</b> A person who is satisfied and happy. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> From summer camps where happy children were 'happy campers.' <br><br> <b>Example:</b> Give him a book and a cup of coffee, and he’s a happy camper."
A blessing in disguise,"<b>Meaning:</b> A misfortune that eventually results in something good happening later on. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> First appeared in the 1700s in a hymn, referring to how God's providence often looks like trouble at first. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> Getting fired was a blessing in disguise because I started my own successful business."
A chip on your shoulder,"<b>Meaning:</b> Being angry or resentful because you feel you have been treated unfairly. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> In the 1800s, American boys would place a wood chip on their shoulder and dare others to knock it off to start a fight. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> He’s had a chip on his shoulder ever since he was passed over for the promotion."
A dime a dozen,<b>Meaning:</b> Something very common and not valuable or special. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Refers to goods so plentiful they only cost ten cents for twelve items. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> Generic action movies are a dime a dozen these days.
A hot potato,<b>Meaning:</b> A controversial issue or situation that is awkward or unpleasant to deal with. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Based on the physical difficulty of holding a potato straight from a fire. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> The new gun control legislation has become a political hot potato.
A piece of cake,<b>Meaning:</b> Something that is very easy to accomplish. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> From 19th-century 'cake walks' where the prize was a cake and the competition was easy. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> Don't worry about the driving test; it’s a piece of cake.
Bite off more than you can chew,<b>Meaning:</b> To take on a task that is way too big or difficult to handle. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Refers to taking a bite of food so large it cannot be chewed or swallowed easily. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> I think I bit off more than I could chew by taking five classes this semester.
Butterflies in your stomach,<b>Meaning:</b> To feel very nervous or anxious about something. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Describes the fluttery physical sensation caused by adrenaline and anxiety. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> I always get butterflies in my stomach before a big presentation.
By the skin of your teeth,"<b>Meaning:</b> To barely succeed or escape a disaster. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> From the biblical Book of Job, implying a margin of error as thin as the non-existent skin on teeth. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> He passed the math test by the skin of his teeth."
Caught red-handed,<b>Meaning:</b> Caught in the act of doing something wrong or illegal. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> From old laws where a person was only guilty of poaching if caught with the victim's blood still on their hands. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> The thief was caught red-handed as he was climbing out the window.
Chip off the old block,<b>Meaning:</b> A person who is very similar in character or appearance to one of their parents. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Comparing a person to a small piece of wood (the child) taken from a larger log (the parent). <br><br> <b>Example:</b> Mark is a chip off the old block; he has his father’s sense of humor.
Cost a pretty penny,"<b>Meaning:</b> To be very expensive. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> From 18th-century Britain, referring to a large sum of money. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> That new kitchen renovation must have cost a pretty penny."
Don't put all your eggs in one basket,"<b>Meaning:</b> Don't risk everything you have on a single venture or idea. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> A literal warning; if you drop the basket, you lose every egg you own. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> Diversify your investments; don't put all your eggs in one basket."
Down to earth,"<b>Meaning:</b> Practical, humble, and realistic. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> A metaphor for someone who stays connected to the ground rather than having their 'head in the clouds.' <br><br> <b>Example:</b> Despite being a famous actor, he is incredibly down to earth."
Drive someone up the wall,<b>Meaning:</b> To annoy or irritate someone intensely. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Suggests making someone so crazy they want to climb the walls to escape the annoyance. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> That loud clicking sound is driving me up the wall.
Elephant in the room,"<b>Meaning:</b> A major problem or controversial issue that is present but everyone is ignoring. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Based on the idea that an elephant is too big to be missed, yet people pretend it isn't there. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> No one wanted to talk about the company's debt, which was the elephant in the room."
From scratch,<b>Meaning:</b> From the very beginning using basic ingredients or materials. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> From sports like racing where a starting line was scratched into the dirt. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> He built the entire computer from scratch.
Get out of hand,"<b>Meaning:</b> To lose control of a situation. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> From horse riding; if the reins leave the rider's hands, the horse is out of control. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> The protest started peacefully, but things quickly got out of hand."
Go the extra mile,"<b>Meaning:</b> To do more than what is strictly required or expected. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> From the Bible; Roman law allowed soldiers to force civilians to carry gear for one mile, and Jesus suggested carrying it for two. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> Our teacher always goes the extra mile to make sure every student understands."
Hit the sack,<b>Meaning:</b> To go to bed or go to sleep. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Refers to old mattresses that were essentially sacks filled with straw or hay. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> I’m exhausted; it’s time for me to hit the sack.
Jump on the bandwagon,"<b>Meaning:</b> To join a popular trend or activity to become successful or popular. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> From 19th-century political parades where people would literally jump on a wagon carrying a band. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> Now that the team is winning, everyone is jumping on the bandwagon."
Like a house on fire,"<b>Meaning:</b> To get along with someone very quickly and very well. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Refers to how quickly a fire spreads through a wooden house. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> I introduced my two friends, and they got on like a house on fire."
Long story short,"<b>Meaning:</b> Used when you are leaving out details to get to the main point of a story quickly. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> A literal instruction to shorten a long narrative. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> Long story short, we missed the flight and had to drive."
Loose cannon,<b>Meaning:</b> A person who is unpredictable and likely to cause trouble. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> From old warships where a cannon that came loose from its moorings would roll dangerously across the deck. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> The CEO is a bit of a loose cannon; you never know what he’ll say to the press.
Pull yourself together,"<b>Meaning:</b> To calm down and recover your self-control after being upset or angry. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> A metaphor for collecting your scattered emotions and 'assembling' yourself. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> I know you’re stressed, but you need to pull yourself together for the interview."
Put someone on the spot,<b>Meaning:</b> To force someone to answer a difficult question or make a decision immediately. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Likely from old practices of placing someone in a specific place for public questioning or execution. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> The teacher really put me on the spot when she asked me for the answer in front of everyone.
Rock the boat,"<b>Meaning:</b> To do or say something that upsets a stable situation or causes trouble. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> A literal metaphor; moving around in a small boat can make it tip over. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> Everyone is happy with the current schedule, so please don't rock the boat."
Sit on the fence,"<b>Meaning:</b> To avoid making a decision or choosing a side in an argument. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> A metaphor for someone sitting on a boundary between two sides, belonging to neither. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> You can't sit on the fence forever; you need to choose which candidate to support."
To make matters worse,"<b>Meaning:</b> To do something that makes a bad situation even more difficult. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> A literal description of escalating a problem. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> I lost my keys, and to make matters worse, it started raining."
Walking on eggshells,<b>Meaning:</b> To be extremely cautious about what you say or do around someone to avoid upsetting them. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> A metaphor for the extreme care needed to walk on delicate eggshells without breaking them. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> Everyone at the office is walking on eggshells because the boss is in a terrible mood.
Wrap your head around something,"<b>Meaning:</b> To understand something complicated or strange. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> A metaphor for your mind physically surrounding an idea to contain it. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> I’m trying to wrap my head around the new tax laws, but they are so confusing."
Actions speak louder than words,"<b>Meaning:</b> What someone does is more important than what they say they will do. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Dates back to the 1600s, popularized in the 1800s in American politics. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> He says he loves me, but he never helps around the house; actions speak louder than words."
Add fuel to the fire,<b>Meaning:</b> To make a bad situation even worse or more intense. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> A literal metaphor for how fire grows when fuel is added. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> Bringing up their old argument only added fuel to the fire.
As right as rain,"<b>Meaning:</b> Feeling perfectly healthy or correct. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Uncertain, but possibly because rain is 'right' and necessary for the earth. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> I was sick last week, but I'm as right as rain now."
Apple of discord,<b>Meaning:</b> A cause of envy or quarrel. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> From the Greek myth of the Golden Apple of Discord which led to the Trojan War. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> The ancestral property became an apple of discord between the two brothers.
At arm's length,<b>Meaning:</b> To keep someone at a distance to avoid intimacy or conflict. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> A physical metaphor for keeping someone far enough away so they cannot touch or influence you. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> It is better to keep dishonest people at arm's length.
Bed of thorns,"<b>Meaning:</b> A situation full of difficulties and sorrows. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> The opposite of 'bed of roses,' symbolizing pain and hardship. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> The life of a refugee is often a bed of thorns."
By hook or by crook,"<b>Meaning:</b> By any means possible, fair or unfair. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Refers to medieval forest laws where peasants could only gather firewood they could reach with a shepherd’s hook or a reaper’s crook. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> He decided to win the election by hook or by crook."
Cats and dogs,"<b>Meaning:</b> To rain very heavily. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Uncertain, but possibly from old street drainage systems where dead animals were washed away during heavy storms. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> We couldn't go out because it was raining cats and dogs."
Fair weather friend,"<b>Meaning:</b> A person who is a friend only when times are good. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> A nautical metaphor; just as fair weather makes sailing easy, these friends only stick around when life is easy. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> I thought he was a close friend, but he turned out to be a fair weather friend when I lost my money."
Feather in one's cap,<b>Meaning:</b> An achievement to be proud of. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> From the custom of warriors adding a feather to their headgear for every enemy they killed. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> Winning the national debate was a real feather in his cap.
Fly in the ointment,"<b>Meaning:</b> A small defect that spoils something valuable or pleasant. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> From the Bible (Ecclesiastes 10:1), referring to dead flies making a perfumer's ointment stink. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> The picnic was perfect; the only fly in the ointment was the mosquitoes."
Kith and kin,"<b>Meaning:</b> Friends and relatives. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> 'Kith' originally meant 'known people' (friends), and 'Kin' meant family. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> He invited all his kith and kin to the wedding."
Maiden speech,"<b>Meaning:</b> The first speech made by a person, typically in parliament. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> 'Maiden' signifies 'first' or 'original' in this context. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> Her maiden speech in the parliament was highly praised by the senior leaders."
Nip in the bud,"<b>Meaning:</b> To stop something at an early stage before it can develop. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> A gardening metaphor; if you nip a flower bud, the flower will never grow. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> We must nip this bad habit in the bud before it becomes a serious problem."
Pros and cons,<b>Meaning:</b> The advantages and disadvantages of something. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Abbreviation of the Latin phrase 'pro et contra' (for and against). <br><br> <b>Example:</b> We weighed the pros and cons before deciding to move to the city.
Bail someone out,"<b>Meaning:</b> To rescue someone from a difficult situation, often financial. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Removing water from a sinking boat using a bucket (bailing). <br><br> <b>Example:</b> His parents had to bail him out after he overspent on his credit card."
Beat someone to the punch,"<b>Meaning:</b> To do or achieve something before someone else does. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> From boxing; landing a hit before your opponent can. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> I wanted to buy that house, but someone else beat me to the punch."
Bend the rules,"<b>Meaning:</b> To allow an exception to a rule without officially changing it. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Treating a rigid rule as if it were flexible. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> The library is closed, but since you're almost done, I'll bend the rules and give you five more minutes."
Best of both worlds,<b>Meaning:</b> A situation where you can enjoy the advantages of two very different things at the same time. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> A 19th-century expression. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> Working from home gives her the best of both worlds: a career and time with family.
Blindside someone,<b>Meaning:</b> To surprise someone with a negative situation they didn't see coming. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> From American football; being hit from a direction the player cannot see. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> The sudden layoffs completely blindsided the staff.
"Blood, sweat, and tears","<b>Meaning:</b> Extreme hard work and effort. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Popularized by Winston Churchill during WWII. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> I put blood, sweat, and tears into building this company."
Bottom of the barrel,<b>Meaning:</b> The lowest quality or the last remaining options. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> The sediment and worst bits of food found at the bottom of a storage barrel. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> These applicants are the bottom of the barrel; we need to find better candidates.
Bring to the table,<b>Meaning:</b> To provide something of value to a group or negotiation. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Bringing food to a communal meal. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> She brings a lot of marketing experience to the table.
Buy time,<b>Meaning:</b> To delay an event or decision to gain an advantage. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Treating time as a commodity that can be purchased through delay. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> I told them the check was in the mail to buy us some time.
Card up your sleeve,"<b>Meaning:</b> A secret advantage or plan. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Cheating at cards by hiding a strong card in your clothing. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> If they won't lower the price, I still have a card up my sleeve."
Change of pace,<b>Meaning:</b> A temporary change from one's normal routine. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Shifting the speed of a journey. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> Going to the mountains was a nice change of pace from the city.
Chip on your shoulder,<b>Meaning:</b> To be easily angered or have a persistent grudge. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Challenging someone to a fight by daring them to knock a wood chip off your shoulder. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> He’s had a chip on his shoulder ever since he was fired.
Climb the corporate ladder,<b>Meaning:</b> To advance in a company's hierarchy. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> A metaphor for the vertical structure of a business. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> He worked long hours to climb the corporate ladder.
"Close, but no cigar","<b>Meaning:</b> Almost successful but failing in the end. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Fairground games used to give cigars as prizes for winning. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> You almost beat the record—close, but no cigar."
Corner the market,<b>Meaning:</b> To gain enough control of a commodity or service to set the price. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Forcing competitors into a 'corner' where they cannot move. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> That company has cornered the market on smartphone screens.
Dead heat,"<b>Meaning:</b> A competition that ends in a perfect tie. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Horse racing; a 'heat' is a race that leaves the horses 'dead' (exhausted). <br><br> <b>Example:</b> The election ended in a dead heat, requiring a recount."
Deep pockets,<b>Meaning:</b> Having a lot of money or financial resources. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> A metaphor for a person who can reach deep into their pockets for cash. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> We need an investor with deep pockets to fund this project.
Deliver the goods,"<b>Meaning:</b> To do what you promised or what is expected of you. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Fulfilling a shipping order. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> He talks a lot, but he always delivers the goods."
Dirt cheap,<b>Meaning:</b> Extremely inexpensive. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Dirt is valueless and available everywhere. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> I got this shirt dirt cheap at the clearance sale.
Drag your feet,<b>Meaning:</b> To act slowly or reluctantly. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Physically walking slowly to show you don't want to go. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> The government is dragging its feet on passing the new law.
Eat your words,"<b>Meaning:</b> To admit that what you said was wrong. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Taking back your speech. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> He said I'd never win, but he had to eat his words when I got the trophy."
Fast track,"<b>Meaning:</b> The quickest route to success or a goal. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> A dry, firm horse racing track that allows for high speeds. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> She is on the fast track to becoming a partner at the firm."
Fill someone's shoes,<b>Meaning:</b> To take over someone's role and do it as well as they did. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> A metaphor for taking on another person's path. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> The old manager was great; it will be hard to fill his shoes.
Fine-tune,<b>Meaning:</b> To make small adjustments to make something work perfectly. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Tuning a musical instrument or radio frequency. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> We just need to fine-tune the engine before the race.
Gain ground,<b>Meaning:</b> To make progress or become more successful. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Military; moving forward and taking territory from the enemy. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> Our new product is finally starting to gain ground against the competition.
Get a second wind,"<b>Meaning:</b> To get a new burst of energy after being tired. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Running; when breathing becomes easier after the initial struggle. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> I was exhausted, but after a snack, I got a second wind."
Get wind of,<b>Meaning:</b> To hear a rumor or secret information. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Hunting; an animal smelling a predator on the wind. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> The reporters got wind of the scandal before it was announced.
Hear it on the grapevine,<b>Meaning:</b> To hear news through gossip or informal communication. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Civil War telegraph lines that looked like tangled vines. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> I heard on the grapevine that the boss is retiring.
In the same boat,"<b>Meaning:</b> In the same difficult situation as others. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> If a boat sinks, everyone on board is in trouble together. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> We all lost our jobs today, so we're in the same boat."
Last resort,<b>Meaning:</b> The final option used only when everything else has failed. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Legal term for the final court of appeal. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> We will go to court only as a last resort.
Raise the bar,<b>Meaning:</b> To increase standards or expectations. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> High jump or pole vaulting; making the jump harder. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> Her excellent presentation has really raised the bar for the rest of us.
A perfect storm,<b>Meaning:</b> A particularly bad situation caused by a combination of unfavorable circumstances. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Meteorological; when several weather systems collide to create a massive storm. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> High interest rates and a supply shortage created a perfect storm for the housing market.
All roads lead to Rome,"<b>Meaning:</b> There are many different ways to reach the same goal. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> From the ancient Roman Empire, where the road system was designed to radiate from the capital. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> Whether we use this software or that one, all roads lead to Rome; the data stays the same."
At the end of one's rope,"<b>Meaning:</b> To have no more patience or strength left to deal with a situation. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Tethering an animal; if it reaches the end of the rope, it can go no further. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> I've been trying to fix this car for hours and I'm at the end of my rope."
Blow hot and cold,"<b>Meaning:</b> To keep changing your attitude or opinion about something. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> One of Aesop's fables where a man blows on his hands to warm them and on soup to cool it. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> One day he loves the idea, the next he hates it; he really blows hot and cold."
Buy the farm,"<b>Meaning:</b> To die, especially in battle or an accident. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> WWII slang; if a pilot crashed on a farm, the insurance would 'buy the farm' (pay it off) for the farmer. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> It’s a dangerous job; many pilots have bought the farm on that route."
Cast the first stone,"<b>Meaning:</b> To be the first to criticize someone. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> From the Bible (John 8:7) regarding judging others. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> Before you criticize her for being late, remember you were late yesterday; don't cast the first stone."
Crack the whip,<b>Meaning:</b> To use your authority to make people work harder. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> From horse or ox drivers using a whip to speed up the animals. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> The manager had to crack the whip to get the project finished.
Dog in the manger,"<b>Meaning:</b> A person who prevents others from using something they do not need themselves. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> One of Aesop's fables where a dog sits in a manger (hay bin) and stops the ox from eating the hay. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> He doesn't even use the extra office, but won't let anyone else sit there; he’s a real dog in the manger."
Eye of the hurricane,"<b>Meaning:</b> A period of calm in the middle of a chaotic situation. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> The center of a tropical storm is eerily calm. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> The morning was peaceful, but it was just the eye of the hurricane before the meeting started."
Fool's paradise,"<b>Meaning:</b> A state of happiness based on false hope or ignorance. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> 15th-century literature. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> They think the company is doing well, but they're living in a fool's paradise."
Get a raw deal,<b>Meaning:</b> To be treated unfairly. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> A 'deal' (transaction) that is unfinished or poorly handled. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> He really got a raw deal when they fired him right before his bonus.
Gild the lily,"<b>Meaning:</b> To try to improve something that is already beautiful or perfect, often ruining it. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Shakespeare (King John); though the original quote was 'paint the lily.' <br><br> <b>Example:</b> The house is perfect; adding that fountain would just be gilding the lily."
Go for the jugular,"<b>Meaning:</b> To attack an opponent's weakest or most sensitive point. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Predators attacking the throat (jugular vein) of their prey. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> In the debate, he went for the jugular as soon as his opponent made a mistake."
Hard to swallow,<b>Meaning:</b> Difficult to believe or accept. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Metaphor for a large or unpleasant piece of food. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> His excuse was a bit hard to swallow.
In the doldrums,"<b>Meaning:</b> Lacking energy, stagnant, or depressed. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> An area of the ocean near the equator where there is often no wind to move ships. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> The economy has been in the doldrums for months."
It's a small world,<b>Meaning:</b> A comment on a surprising coincidence. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> A common phrase regarding interconnectedness. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> You know my brother? It’s a small world!
Lead someone down the garden path,"<b>Meaning:</b> To deceive or mislead someone. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Leading someone into a maze or a private area where they lose their way. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> He promised us a huge profit, but he was just leading us down the garden path."
Nip it in the bud,<b>Meaning:</b> To stop a problem while it is still small. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Gardening; cutting off a flower bud before it can bloom. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> We need to nip this rumor in the bud before it spreads.
Rain on your parade,"<b>Meaning:</b> To spoil someone's plans or happiness. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> A literal image of bad weather ruining an event. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> I hate to rain on your parade, but the concert is canceled."
Right off the bat,<b>Meaning:</b> Immediately. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Baseball; the moment the ball hits the bat. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> I knew right off the bat that we would be friends.
Backhanded compliment,<b>Meaning:</b> A comment that seems like praise but is actually an insult. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Using the back of the hand (weak/clumsy) rather than the palm (strong/honest). <br><br> <b>Example:</b> Saying 'you look surprisingly good today' is a backhanded compliment.
Behind someone's back,"<b>Meaning:</b> Doing something without someone's knowledge, often in a dishonest way. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Acting where the person cannot see you. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> I can't believe he sold the car behind my back."
Bite the hand that feeds you,<b>Meaning:</b> To treat someone badly who has helped you. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> A literal warning about a dog biting its owner. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> Don't insult your boss; you shouldn't bite the hand that feeds you.
Bone of contention,<b>Meaning:</b> A subject or issue that causes a long-standing argument. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Two dogs fighting over a single bone. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> The division of the inheritance was a major bone of contention between the siblings.
Can't judge a book by its cover,"<b>Meaning:</b> Decisions shouldn't be made based on outward appearances. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> A 19th-century proverb. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> He looks scary, but he's actually very kind; you can't judge a book by its cover."
Carry the weight of the world,"<b>Meaning:</b> To feel like you have a huge amount of responsibility or stress. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> From the Greek myth of Atlas, who held up the sky. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> Ever since his father died, he feels like he's carrying the weight of the world."
Clean slate,"<b>Meaning:</b> A fresh start with no past mistakes being held against you. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Wiping a chalk slate clean in a schoolroom. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> After serving his sentence, he wanted a clean slate."
Don't count your chickens,"<b>Meaning:</b> Don't make plans based on events that haven't happened yet. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Not all eggs hatch into chickens. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> You haven't won the lottery yet, so don't count your chickens."
Drastic times call for drastic measures,"<b>Meaning:</b> When a situation is very bad, you must take extreme actions. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Likely from the Greek physician Hippocrates. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> The company was failing, so they fired half the staff; drastic times call for drastic measures."
Drink like a fish,<b>Meaning:</b> To drink a lot of alcohol. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> A literal comparison to a fish's mouth moving. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> He really started to drink like a fish after he lost his job.
Drop in the bucket,<b>Meaning:</b> A tiny amount compared to what is needed. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> From the Bible. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> The $5 donation is just a drop in the bucket.
Dumb luck,"<b>Meaning:</b> Success achieved purely by chance, not skill. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> 'Dumb' meaning silent or senseless. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> Winning the game was just dumb luck."
Eat crow,"<b>Meaning:</b> To admit a humiliating mistake. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Crows are unpleasant to eat, just as admitting failure is unpleasant. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> He had to eat crow when his prediction turned out to be wrong."
Everything but the kitchen sink,<b>Meaning:</b> Almost everything imaginable. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> From WWII; implies taking everything portable. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> She packed everything but the kitchen sink for the weekend.
Eye for an eye,"<b>Meaning:</b> A punishment that matches the crime. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Ancient law of retribution (lex talionis). <br><br> <b>Example:</b> He broke my phone, so I broke his—an eye for an eye."
Fall on your sword,<b>Meaning:</b> To take the blame and resign from a position. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Roman generals committing suicide after defeat. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> The CEO fell on his sword after the scandal was revealed.
Go back to the drawing board,"<b>Meaning:</b> To start over after a failure. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Design and engineering. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> The plan didn't work, so it's back to the drawing board."
Heart of gold,<b>Meaning:</b> Very kind and generous. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Gold as a symbol of purity. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> She has a heart of gold.
High on the hog,<b>Meaning:</b> Living in luxury. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> The best meat is on the top of the pig. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> They've been living high on the hog since they won.
Small world,<b>Meaning:</b> Surprising coincidence. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Common phrase. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> You know her too? Small world!
Above board,<b>Meaning:</b> Honest and open; not secret. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> From card games where players kept their hands above the table (board) to prove they weren't cheating. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> The deal was completely above board.
Ace up your sleeve,<b>Meaning:</b> A secret advantage or resource kept in reserve. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> From dishonest card players hiding an Ace in their clothing. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> I have an ace up my sleeve for the final negotiation.
Across the board,"<b>Meaning:</b> Applying to all members or categories of a group. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> From horse racing, where a bet on a horse to win, place, or show is marked across the bookmaker's board. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> The company gave a 5% raise across the board."
All systems go,"<b>Meaning:</b> Everything is ready to start. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> From NASA rocket launches when every department confirms readiness. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> The new store opens tomorrow, and it’s all systems go."
At death's door,<b>Meaning:</b> Very near death or extremely ill. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> A person being so sick they are standing at the entrance to the afterlife. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> He looked like he was at death's door when he had the flu.
Axe to grind,<b>Meaning:</b> To have a strong personal opinion or a hidden motive for doing something. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> From a story about a boy tricked into sharpening an axe; now implies someone has a selfish reason to be involved. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> He’s only helping us because he has an axe to grind with the boss.
Back to the wall,"<b>Meaning:</b> In a defensive or desperate position with no room to maneuver. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Military; if you have a wall behind you, you cannot retreat further and must fight. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> With the bank threatening to close the shop, he has his back to the wall."
Bank on it,<b>Meaning:</b> To be so sure of something that you would bet your money on it. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Financial; the bank is seen as a reliable and safe institution. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> It will rain tonight; you can bank on it.
"Beg, borrow, or steal","<b>Meaning:</b> To do whatever is necessary to get something. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> A list of all ways—honest and dishonest—to acquire an item. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> I need that ticket; I’ll beg, borrow, or steal to get it."
Bite your head off,<b>Meaning:</b> To speak to someone in a very angry and sudden way. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> An exaggeration of a person's aggressive verbal reaction. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> I only asked a question; you don't have to bite my head off.
Bitter pill to swallow,<b>Meaning:</b> An unpleasant fact that is difficult to accept. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Medicine that tastes bad but must be taken for health. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> Losing the championship was a bitter pill to swallow.
Blue blood,"<b>Meaning:</b> Someone who comes from a noble or aristocratic family. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> From the Spanish 'sangre azul'; aristocrats were so pale their veins looked blue. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> She acts like blue blood, even though she grew up in a normal house."
Bolt from the blue,<b>Meaning:</b> A sudden and unexpected event. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> A bolt of lightning appearing in a clear blue sky. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> His resignation was a bolt from the blue.
Bottom line,<b>Meaning:</b> The most important point or the final profit/loss of a business. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> The final line on a financial statement showing the total. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> The bottom line is that we are losing money.
Burn a hole in your pocket,"<b>Meaning:</b> Money that you are very eager to spend. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> The idea that money is so 'hot' it burns through the pocket until it is gone. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> As soon as he gets paid, the money burns a hole in his pocket."
Butter someone up,<b>Meaning:</b> To be very nice to someone so that they will do you a favor. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> An ancient Indian custom of throwing balls of butter at statues of gods to seek favor. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> He’s trying to butter up the boss to get a promotion.
Change your tune,"<b>Meaning:</b> To change your opinion or the way you talk about something. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> A musician changing the melody they are playing. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> He used to hate that car, but he changed his tune when he drove it."
Chew someone out,<b>Meaning:</b> To scold someone severely. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Military slang; a metaphor for 'eating' someone up with words. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> The coach chewed the team out for losing.
Clutch at straws,"<b>Meaning:</b> To try any possible thing to save oneself from a bad situation, even if it is unlikely to work. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> A drowning person trying to grab onto floating pieces of straw. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> He has no evidence; he’s just clutching at straws."
Cold comfort,<b>Meaning:</b> Very little or no comfort at all. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Comfort that is 'cold' feels unfriendly and unhelpful. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> Telling me everyone failed the test was cold comfort.
Cooking the books,<b>Meaning:</b> Manipulating financial records illegally to hide a loss or steal money. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> 'Cooked' means altered or prepared; 'books' refers to accounting ledgers. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> The accountant was arrested for cooking the books.
Crack a book,<b>Meaning:</b> To open a book to study. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> The sound of a new book's spine cracking when opened for the first time. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> He failed because he didn't crack a book all semester.
Cream of the crop,<b>Meaning:</b> The very best of a group. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> The cream rises to the top of the milk; the best part of the harvest. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> These students are the cream of the crop.
Cut a long story short,"<b>Meaning:</b> To get to the point. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> A literal summary request. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> To cut a long story short, we missed the train."
Cut to the bone,<b>Meaning:</b> To reduce something (like a budget) to the lowest possible level. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> A metaphor for cutting through meat until you reach the bone. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> Our funding has been cut to the bone.
Dance to someone's tune,<b>Meaning:</b> To do exactly what someone else wants you to do. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> A metaphor for a puppet or dancer following a musician's lead. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> I’m not going to dance to his tune anymore.
Days are numbered,<b>Meaning:</b> Someone or something will not last much longer. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> From the Bible (Daniel 5:26); God has 'numbered' the days of a kingdom. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> This old computer’s days are numbered.
Diamonds in the rough,"<b>Meaning:</b> Someone who has great potential but lacks social grace or training. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Natural diamonds look like ordinary rocks until they are cut and polished. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> He’s a bit rude, but he’s a diamond in the rough."
Dime a dozen,<b>Meaning:</b> Very common and easy to find; of little value. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> When an item is so cheap you can get twelve for ten cents (a dime). <br><br> <b>Example:</b> Apps like this are a dime a dozen.
Do a 180,"<b>Meaning:</b> To completely change your opinion or direction. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> A 180-degree turn is a half-circle, facing the opposite way. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> She used to love coffee, but she did a 180 and now only drinks tea."
Down the drain,<b>Meaning:</b> Wasted or lost completely. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> A metaphor for water or money disappearing into a sewer. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> All our hard work went down the drain.
Drown your sorrows,<b>Meaning:</b> To drink a lot of alcohol to forget your problems. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> A metaphor for 'submerging' sadness in liquid. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> He went to the bar to drown his sorrows after the breakup.
Every man for himself,"<b>Meaning:</b> Everyone must look after their own interests because no one else will help. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Often associated with emergencies like a sinking ship. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> When the fire alarm went off, it was every man for himself."
Eye-opener,<b>Meaning:</b> A situation that makes you realize something surprising or shocking. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> A metaphor for literally opening the eyes to a truth. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> Visiting the slums was a real eye-opener for the students.
Feather your nest,"<b>Meaning:</b> To make yourself rich, often dishonestly. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> See previous explanation. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> He was caught feathering his nest with government money."
Fight fire with fire,"<b>Meaning:</b> To use the same methods as your opponent to defeat them. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> From firefighting; a small fire is sometimes used to burn fuel before a larger fire reaches it. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> They are using aggressive ads, so we have to fight fire with fire."
Fishy,"<b>Meaning:</b> Suspicious or dishonest. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Fresh fish has no smell, but old or 'bad' fish smells strongly; therefore, a 'smell' indicates something is wrong. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> Something about his story sounds fishy."
Fit for a king,<b>Meaning:</b> Of very high quality; excellent. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Worthy of being served to royalty. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> This meal is fit for a king.
Fool's errand,<b>Meaning:</b> A task that is useless or impossible to complete. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> A prank where a person is sent to find something that doesn't exist. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> Looking for a gas station in this desert is a fool's errand.
For a song,<b>Meaning:</b> Very cheaply. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> From a time when street performers would sing for a single coin. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> I bought this antique table for a song.
Full steam ahead,"<b>Meaning:</b> To proceed with maximum energy and speed. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> From steamships or trains using the highest pressure to move as fast as possible. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> Once we get the permit, it's full steam ahead."
Give and take,<b>Meaning:</b> Compromise; a situation where both people make concessions. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> A literal exchange of 'giving' something and 'taking' something else. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> Every marriage requires some give and take.
Go the distance,<b>Meaning:</b> To finish something even though it is very difficult. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> From boxing; a fighter who lasts for all the scheduled rounds. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> I don't know if I can go the distance with this marathon.
Hands-down,<b>Meaning:</b> Easily and without question. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> From horse racing; a jockey who is so far ahead they can drop their hands (reins) and still win. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> She is hands-down the best singer in the competition.
Hard up,<b>Meaning:</b> Short of money. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Nautical; turning the tiller 'hard up' against the wind when in trouble. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> He’s a bit hard up since he lost his job.
Heads up,"<b>Meaning:</b> A warning that something is about to happen. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> A literal instruction to look up and see a danger. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> Just a heads up, the meeting has been moved."
Hit pay dirt,"<b>Meaning:</b> To find something very valuable or successful. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> From gold mining; finding the 'dirt' that contains gold. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> After months of research, we finally hit pay dirt."
In the black,<b>Meaning:</b> Making a profit; not in debt. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Accountants used black ink for profits and red ink for losses. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> The company is finally in the black.
Keep a weather eye out,<b>Meaning:</b> To watch carefully for signs of trouble. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Nautical; sailors watching the horizon for changes in the weather. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> Keep a weather eye out for any changes in the market.
Learn the ropes,<b>Meaning:</b> To learn how to do a job. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> See previous explanation. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> It took me a week to learn the ropes.
Live on borrowed time,<b>Meaning:</b> To survive longer than expected. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> The idea that you have used up your 'allotted' time and are now using time you don't 'own.' <br><br> <b>Example:</b> This old car is living on borrowed time.
Bark is worse than their bite,"<b>Meaning:</b> Someone who sounds threatening or angry but is actually harmless. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Comparing a loud, barking dog that doesn't actually attack. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> Don't worry about the manager's shouting; his bark is worse than his bite."
Beat a dead horse,<b>Meaning:</b> To waste time doing something that has already been finished or decided. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> A literal image of trying to make a dead horse run by whipping it. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> We've already decided to cancel the project; stop trying to beat a dead horse.
Burst your bubble,"<b>Meaning:</b> To ruin someone's happiness or illusions by telling them the truth. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> A metaphor for a fragile, beautiful soap bubble being popped. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> I hate to burst your bubble, but the concert is already sold out."
Early bird gets the worm,<b>Meaning:</b> The person who arrives or acts first will have the best chance of success. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> A literal observation of birds hunting for worms at dawn. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> I got to the sale at 6 AM; the early bird gets the worm.
Fine line,<b>Meaning:</b> A very small difference between two things. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> A metaphor for a thin boundary that is easy to cross. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> There's a fine line between being confident and being arrogant.
Follow your nose,"<b>Meaning:</b> To go straight ahead or trust your instincts. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> A literal instruction to move in the direction you are facing. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> To find the bakery, just follow your nose."
Get something off your chest,<b>Meaning:</b> To tell someone something that has been worrying you. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> A metaphor for removing a heavy weight of guilt or anxiety from your body. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> I need to get something off my chest: I was the one who broke the vase.
Get under someone's skin,<b>Meaning:</b> To annoy or irritate someone intensely. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> A metaphor for a parasite or splinter that causes constant irritation. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> His constant whistling really gets under my skin.
Get your act together,<b>Meaning:</b> To organize yourself and your life so that you can be successful. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> From theater; a performer needs to have their 'act' ready before going on stage. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> You need to get your act together if you want to pass this class.
Give someone a piece of your mind,<b>Meaning:</b> To speak angrily to someone about something they have done wrong. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> The idea of 'sharing' your angry thoughts with someone else. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> I’m going to give the manager a piece of my mind about this service.
Go out on a limb,"<b>Meaning:</b> To take a risk or do something that puts you in a vulnerable position. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Climbing a tree; the further you go on a limb (branch), the more likely it is to break. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> I’m going to go out on a limb and say that this plan will work."
Greased lightning,<b>Meaning:</b> Something that moves extremely fast. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Lightning is fast; adding 'grease' implies making it even smoother and faster. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> He ran across the field like greased lightning.
Have your head in the clouds,<b>Meaning:</b> To be unaware of what is happening around you because you are daydreaming. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> A literal image of someone standing so tall they are in the clouds and can't see the ground. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> He always has his head in the clouds and never finishes his work.
In the hot seat,<b>Meaning:</b> In a difficult position where you have to answer tough questions or make hard decisions. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Originally slang for the electric chair; later became a metaphor for being under pressure. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> The CEO was in the hot seat during the press conference.
It's not rocket science,<b>Meaning:</b> Used to say that something is not difficult to understand. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Comparing a task to the high complexity of aerospace engineering. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> Just follow the instructions; it’s not rocket science.
Knock someone's socks off,<b>Meaning:</b> To impress someone very much. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> An exaggeration of a physical reaction to a surprise. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> Her singing performance will knock your socks off.
Let your hair down,<b>Meaning:</b> To relax and behave in an informal way. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> From a time when women wore their hair up in formal styles and only let it down at home. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> It’s a party! Come on and let your hair down.
Like a moth to a flame,<b>Meaning:</b> Being strongly attracted to something that is dangerous. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> A literal observation of insects flying into fire and dying. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> He’s attracted to trouble like a moth to a flame.
Night owl,<b>Meaning:</b> Someone who stays up late at night. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Owls are nocturnal animals that are active at night. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> I’ve always been a night owl; I do my best work after midnight.
No use crying over spilled milk,"<b>Meaning:</b> Don't worry about things that have already happened and can't be changed. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Once milk is spilled, it's gone; crying won't bring it back. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> You missed the bus; there's no use crying over spilled milk."
On a silver platter,<b>Meaning:</b> To get something easily without having to work for it. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> A metaphor for being served like royalty. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> He didn't have to work for his money; his father gave it to him on a silver platter.
Pain in the neck,"<b>Meaning:</b> Someone or something that is very annoying. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> A physical metaphor for a constant, nagging ache. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> Doing taxes is a real pain in the neck."
Rain check,"<b>Meaning:</b> A promise to do something at a later date because you can't do it now. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> From baseball; if it rained, spectators were given a 'rain check' for a future game. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> I can't go to dinner tonight; can I take a rain check?"
Right under your nose,<b>Meaning:</b> In a place that is very easy to see. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> A literal description of something being extremely close. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> I spent all day looking for my keys and they were right under my nose on the table.
Roll with the punches,"<b>Meaning:</b> To deal with problems and difficulties as they happen. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> From boxing; moving your body with an opponent's punch to reduce its impact. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> Business is tough right now, but we just have to roll with the punches."
Rome wasn't built in a day,<b>Meaning:</b> Important things take time to achieve. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> A 12th-century French proverb. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> Don't get discouraged by your slow progress; Rome wasn't built in a day.
Second nature,<b>Meaning:</b> Something you can do easily because you have done it so many times. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Habits become so strong they feel like they were part of your natural self from birth. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> Driving is second nature to me now.
Slow and steady wins the race,<b>Meaning:</b> Being consistent is more important than being fast. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> From Aesop's fables; the tortoise and the hare. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> I’m taking my time with this project; slow and steady wins the race.
The apple of my eye,"<b>Meaning:</b> Someone who is very special and loved. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> From the Bible; the 'apple' was the pupil of the eye, which is the most sensitive and precious part. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> My little granddaughter is the apple of my eye."
The early bird gets the worm,<b>Meaning:</b> Acting early leads to success. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> See previous explanation. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> I got the best seat because I arrived early; the early bird gets the worm.
The grass is always greener,<b>Meaning:</b> Thinking others have it better. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> See previous explanation. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> He’s always unhappy with his job; the grass is always greener on the other side.
A lot on one's plate,"<b>Meaning:</b> To be very busy with many tasks or responsibilities. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> A metaphor for a dinner plate piled so high with food that it is difficult to manage. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> I'd love to help with the fundraiser, but I have a lot on my plate right now."
All hands on deck,"<b>Meaning:</b> Everyone must help or work together to solve a problem. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Nautical; a command for all sailors to come to the deck to handle an emergency. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> We have a huge deadline tomorrow, so it's all hands on deck."
Blue-collar worker,<b>Meaning:</b> Someone who performs manual labor. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Refers to the durable blue fabrics (like denim) traditionally worn by industrial workers. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> My father was a blue-collar worker in a steel mill for thirty years.
Cash cow,<b>Meaning:</b> A business or product that generates a steady and significant profit. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Comparing a profitable business to a cow that can be milked indefinitely. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> Their software division has become the company's cash cow.
Cloud on the horizon,<b>Meaning:</b> A sign of potential trouble in the future. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Weather; a distant cloud indicates a storm may be coming. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> The new tax law is the only cloud on the horizon for our expansion plans.
Cut through the red tape,<b>Meaning:</b> To bypass complicated official rules and processes to get something done. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Legal documents in 17th-century England were bound with red ribbon (tape). <br><br> <b>Example:</b> I hired a lawyer to help us cut through the red tape of the immigration process.
Duck out,<b>Meaning:</b> To leave a place or situation quickly or secretly. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Likely from the way a duck dives underwater to escape. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> I'm going to duck out of the meeting a few minutes early.
Falling on a sword,<b>Meaning:</b> To take the blame for a mistake or to resign to protect others. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> From ancient Roman generals who would commit suicide by falling on their sword after a defeat. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> The CEO fell on his sword to save the company's reputation after the scandal.
Feet to the fire,<b>Meaning:</b> To pressure someone to fulfill a commitment or tell the truth. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> A historical reference to torture used to force confessions. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> The committee is holding the director's feet to the fire regarding the missing budget.
Fifth wheel,<b>Meaning:</b> Someone who is unnecessary or unwelcome in a group. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> A four-wheeled carriage has no use for a fifth wheel. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> I felt like a fifth wheel when I went to dinner with the two couples.
Fly by the seat of your pants,<b>Meaning:</b> To act instinctively without a clear plan or using only the information available. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Early pilots used the physical 'feel' of the plane's vibrations to navigate. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> We didn't have a strategy for the meeting; we were just flying by the seat of our pants.
Follow the money,"<b>Meaning:</b> To investigate the financial trail to find the person responsible for a crime or scandal. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Popularized by the 1976 film 'All the President's Men.' <br><br> <b>Example:</b> To find out who is really behind the project, you just need to follow the money."
Foot the bill,"<b>Meaning:</b> To pay for something, especially when it is expensive. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Adding up the total at the 'foot' (bottom) of a bill and then paying it. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> My parents offered to foot the bill for our wedding."
Gain the upper hand,<b>Meaning:</b> To get into a position of advantage or control. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> From a game where two people move their hands up a bat to see who holds the top. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> Our company has gained the upper hand in the local market.
Go south,"<b>Meaning:</b> To deteriorate, decline, or fail. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> From maps, where 'down' (south) represents a decrease or a fall. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> The deal started to go south when they asked for more money."
Golden handshake,<b>Meaning:</b> A large sum of money given to a high-ranking employee when they leave a company. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> 'Golden' implies wealth; 'handshake' implies a friendly parting. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> The CEO received a multi-million dollar golden handshake when he retired.
Gray area,"<b>Meaning:</b> A situation that is not clear or does not fit into a specific rule. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> The shade between black and white, where things are not clearly defined. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> Whether this is legal or not is a bit of a gray area."
Grease the wheels,<b>Meaning:</b> To make a process happen more smoothly by providing help or money. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Putting grease on a machine's wheels to reduce friction. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> A few phone calls to the right people helped grease the wheels for our permit.
Hammer home,<b>Meaning:</b> To make something very clear by repeating it or emphasizing it strongly. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> A metaphor for driving a nail all the way into wood with repeated strikes. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> The teacher hammered home the importance of checking your work.
Hands are tied,"<b>Meaning:</b> To be unable to help or act because of rules or circumstances. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> A literal image of a person whose hands are bound and cannot move. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> I’d love to give you a discount, but my hands are tied by company policy."
Have a lot of balls in the air,"<b>Meaning:</b> To be dealing with many different tasks at the same time. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> A metaphor for a juggler keeping many balls moving. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> I have a lot of balls in the air right now, so I might be slow to respond."
Heads will roll,"<b>Meaning:</b> People will be severely punished or fired for a mistake. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> A historical reference to execution by beheading. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> If this project fails, heads will roll in the marketing department."
High-flyer,<b>Meaning:</b> Someone who is very successful or has the potential to be. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Comparing a successful person to a bird or plane that flies higher than others. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> She was a high-flyer in the tech world before she started her own company.
Hold the fort,<b>Meaning:</b> To take care of a place or situation while someone else is away. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Military; maintaining a defensive position until reinforcements arrive. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> Can you hold the fort while I go to the bank?
In the driver's seat,"<b>Meaning:</b> To be in control of a situation. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> The person driving the vehicle determines where it goes. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> Now that he's been promoted, he's finally in the driver's seat."
Keep your eye on the prize,"<b>Meaning:</b> To stay focused on your end goal. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Common advice for athletes or competitors. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> The training is hard, but keep your eye on the prize: the championship."
Kick the can down the road,<b>Meaning:</b> To delay dealing with a problem instead of solving it. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> From a children's game; metaphorically moving the problem further away without fixing it. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> The government is just kicking the can down the road instead of fixing the debt.
Land on your feet,"<b>Meaning:</b> To be successful or lucky after a difficult situation. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Refers to the way cats almost always land safely on their feet when they fall. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> He lost his job, but he landed on his feet with an even better one two weeks later."
Learn the hard way,<b>Meaning:</b> To learn something through a bad or painful experience. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Contrast to learning through instruction or advice. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> I learned the hard way that you should always back up your computer files.
Left hand doesn't know what the right hand is doing,"<b>Meaning:</b> A situation where different parts of an organization are not communicating. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> From the Bible (Matthew 6:3), though the meaning has changed from charity to confusion. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> The sales team is offering discounts the billing department won't accept; the left hand doesn't know what the right hand is doing."
Light at the end of the tunnel,"<b>Meaning:</b> A sign that a difficult period is almost over. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> A metaphor for traveling through a dark tunnel and seeing the exit. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> We’ve been working on this for months, but we finally see the light at the end of the tunnel."
Loose ends,<b>Meaning:</b> Small details that haven't been finished yet. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> From sailing; untied ropes hanging from a ship's rigging. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> I just have a few loose ends to tie up before I can leave for vacation.
Mainstay,<b>Meaning:</b> A person or thing that something else depends on. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Nautical; the main rope that supports a ship's mast. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> Tourism is the mainstay of the local economy.
Move the needle,<b>Meaning:</b> To make a noticeable or significant difference in a situation. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> From gauges or meters where a needle moves to show progress. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> We need an advertising campaign that will actually move the needle on sales.
Not going to fly,<b>Meaning:</b> A plan or idea that will not be accepted or will not work. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> A metaphor for a plane that cannot get off the ground. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> Your request for a two-month vacation is just not going to fly.
Odds and ends,"<b>Meaning:</b> Small, unimportant things that are left over. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> From 'odd' (leftover) amounts and 'ends' of fabric or lumber. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> I have a drawer full of odds and ends like old keys and batteries."
Off the clock,<b>Meaning:</b> Not working; during your personal time. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> From 'punching' a time clock to record work hours. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> I can't talk about work right now; I'm off the clock.
Out of the loop,"<b>Meaning:</b> Not part of a group that is kept informed about something. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> See 'In the loop.' <br><br> <b>Example:</b> I was on vacation, so I'm completely out of the loop on the new project."
Paper tiger,<b>Meaning:</b> Someone or something that appears powerful but is actually weak or harmless. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> A Chinese phrase popularized by Mao Zedong. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> Their legal threats are just a paper tiger; they have no real case.
Passing the torch,"<b>Meaning:</b> Handing over a responsibility or a role to someone else. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> From relay races where a literal torch is passed from one runner to another. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> After thirty years, the professor is finally passing the torch to a younger colleague."
Play your cards right,"<b>Meaning:</b> To behave in a way that will give you an advantage or lead to success. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> From card games like poker or bridge. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> If you play your cards right, you could be the next manager."
Poison pill,<b>Meaning:</b> A strategy used by a company to make itself less attractive to a buyer in a hostile takeover. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> From spies who carried a literal poison pill to avoid being captured. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> The company's board of directors is considering a poison pill to stop the buyout.
Put the cart before the horse,<b>Meaning:</b> To do things in the wrong order. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> A horse must be in front to pull a cart. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> Buying furniture before you have a house is putting the cart before the horse.
Raise a red flag,<b>Meaning:</b> To signal that something is wrong or needs attention. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Red flags are traditionally used to signal danger or a stop. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> The fact that he hasn't answered any emails is raising a red flag.
Raking in the money,<b>Meaning:</b> To earn a lot of money very easily. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> A metaphor for gathering money as easily as raking leaves. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> That new movie is raking in the money.
Red-carpet treatment,<b>Meaning:</b> To give someone very special and luxurious treatment. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> From the tradition of rolling out a red carpet for kings and celebrities. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> The airline gave us the red-carpet treatment because it was our honeymoon.
Reinvent the wheel,<b>Meaning:</b> To waste time trying to create something that has already been created. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> The wheel is a perfect invention; trying to 'reinvent' it is pointless. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> We don't need to reinvent the wheel; let's just use the existing template.
Right-hand man,"<b>Meaning:</b> A person's most trusted and helpful assistant. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Historically, the person standing at your right side was in the best position to defend you. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> He’s the CEO's right-hand man."
Rock and a hard place,<b>Meaning:</b> Facing a choice between two equally unpleasant options. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> See 'Between a rock and a hard place.' <br><br> <b>Example:</b> I'm between a rock and a hard place: I either lose my job or work on the weekend.
Run a tight ship,<b>Meaning:</b> To manage an organization or group in a very strict and efficient way. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Nautical; a captain who keeps his ship clean and his crew disciplined. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> My grandmother runs a tight ship; you have to be at the table exactly at 6 PM.
Safe bet,<b>Meaning:</b> Something that is very likely to happen or be successful. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> From gambling; a bet that has very little risk. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> It's a safe bet that it will rain today.
See the big picture,<b>Meaning:</b> To understand the whole situation rather than just the small details. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> A metaphor for stepping back from a painting to see the entire image. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> We need a manager who can see the big picture.
Sever ties,<b>Meaning:</b> To end a relationship or agreement completely. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> A metaphor for cutting the ropes (ties) that connect two things. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> The company decided to sever ties with their supplier.
Shoot from the hip,<b>Meaning:</b> To speak or act quickly and impulsively without thinking. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> From cowboys in the Old West who would fire their guns without aiming properly. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> He’s known for shooting from the hip during press conferences.
Shot in the arm,<b>Meaning:</b> Something that gives you a sudden burst of energy or encouragement. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> A metaphor for a medical injection of a stimulant. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> The new investment was a real shot in the arm for the struggling company.
Show someone the ropes,<b>Meaning:</b> To teach someone how to do a job or activity. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Nautical; teaching a new sailor which ropes to pull. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> Can you show the new employee the ropes?
Sink or swim,<b>Meaning:</b> To fail or succeed by your own efforts without any help. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> A metaphor for being thrown into water and having to figure out how to stay afloat. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> I’m not going to help him; it’s time for him to sink or swim.
Stay the course,<b>Meaning:</b> To continue with a plan even though it is difficult. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Nautical; keeping a ship on its intended path regardless of the weather. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> We need to stay the course if we want to finish the project on time.
From the get-go,<b>Meaning:</b> From the very beginning. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Likely from the 'get-go' or start of a race. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> We knew this project would be difficult from the get-go.
Get a kick out of,<b>Meaning:</b> To enjoy something very much. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> A 'kick' refers to a sudden surge of excitement or adrenaline. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> My dad really gets a kick out of watching old cartoons.
Get down to brass tacks,<b>Meaning:</b> To start talking about the most important facts of a situation. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Likely from haberdashery; brass tacks on a counter were used to measure cloth precisely. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> Stop wasting time and let's get down to brass tacks: how much will this cost?
Give someone the green light,<b>Meaning:</b> To give permission for someone to start something. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> From traffic signals. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> The committee finally gave us the green light to build the park.
Good Samaritan,<b>Meaning:</b> Someone who helps a stranger in trouble. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> From a parable in the Bible about a man from Samaria who helped a traveler. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> A good Samaritan helped me change my tire in the rain.
Have bigger fish to fry,<b>Meaning:</b> To have more important things to do or think about. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> A metaphor for prioritizing a larger task over a small one. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> I'm not worried about the broken pen; I have bigger fish to fry.
Keep someone at arm's length,"<b>Meaning:</b> To avoid being too friendly or close to someone. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Physically pushing someone away so they cannot touch you. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> I like him as a coworker, but I keep him at arm's length socially."
Kill them with kindness,"<b>Meaning:</b> To be extremely nice to someone who is being mean to you. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> A strategy to make an enemy feel ashamed of their own behavior. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> Instead of arguing with the angry customer, she just killed him with kindness."
Live from hand to mouth,<b>Meaning:</b> To have just enough money to buy food and nothing else. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> When food is so scarce it goes directly from the hand to the mouth with no storage. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> Many people are living from hand to mouth during the recession.
Loose lips sink ships,<b>Meaning:</b> Talking too much or sharing secrets can cause a disaster. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> A WWII slogan to warn people that spies might hear their secrets. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> Don't tell anyone about the merger yet; loose lips sink ships.
Smooth operator,"<b>Meaning:</b> Someone who is very skillful at dealing with people or situations, often in a deceptive way. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Popularized by the 1984 Sade song. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> He managed to get the promotion without doing any work; he's a smooth operator."
Snowed under,<b>Meaning:</b> To have too much work or too many things to deal with. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> A metaphor for being buried by a heavy snowfall. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> I'm sorry I didn't call; I've been snowed under with reports.
Stand your ground,<b>Meaning:</b> To refuse to change your opinion or give in to pressure. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Military; refusing to retreat. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> You must stand your ground and demand a refund.
Steal the show,<b>Meaning:</b> To get all the attention and praise in a performance or event. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> From theater; when a supporting actor performs better than the star. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> The little girl's dance really stole the show.
Take it easy,<b>Meaning:</b> To relax or be calm. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> A standard modern instruction to reduce stress. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> Take it easy; there's no need to rush.
Think big,"<b>Meaning:</b> To have ambitious goals and plans. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> A modern motivational phrase. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> If you want to be successful, you have to think big."
To each their own,"<b>Meaning:</b> Everyone has their own preferences or opinions. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> A 16th-century proverb. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> I don't like raw fish, but to each their own."
When in Rome,"<b>Meaning:</b> Follow local customs. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> St. Ambrose. <br><br> <b>Example:</b> They eat late here, so when in Rome..."
Spill the tea,<b>Meaning:</b> To share gossip or juicy secrets. <br><br> <b>Origin:</b> Popularized in drag culture; 'T' stands for 'Truth.' <br><br> <b>Example:</b> I saw them arguing at the party—do you want me to spill the tea?
